# QA Results for quiz-5-padded-final-fixed.md

## Q01

### Grounding

**Q1.** Your supervisor asks you to evaluate Foot's strongest defense for her continued operation of the shelter in violation of the condemnation order under the jurisdiction's applicable common law doctrine (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no reasonable legal alternative was available. <!-- correct -->
(b) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the common law formally exempts charitable actors from criminal liability whenever their actions are motivated by a sincere moral duty to assist vulnerable individuals during severe winter weather conditions.
(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the Model Penal Code, the harm avoided need only be greater than the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that threshold compared to the strict common law requirement of outweighing.
(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she is automatically deemed at fault for creating the emergency solely by operating a shelter in a building that was later condemned, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.
(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the mere existence of general building safety codes and condemnation procedures categorically preempts the necessity defense by foreclosing any judicial balancing of competing harms in this specific case.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Necessity is the correct defense here. Under the common law (Fact 6), a defendant must show: (1) the harm avoided clearly outweighs the harm caused, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) no reasonable legal alternative exists, and (4) the defendant was not at fault in creating the emergency. Option (a) correctly applies these elements. Option (b) is incorrect because the common law contains no blanket exemption for charitable actors or moral motives; the strict necessity elements must be met. Option (c) is incorrect because Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, not the MPC. Option (d) is incorrect because lawfully operating a shelter prior to its condemnation does not automatically constitute culpable fault in creating the weather emergency. Option (e) is incorrect because general building codes are not the type of particularized, specific legislative choice required to preempt the necessity defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, necessity-proportionality, justification-vs-excuse], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students assume moral motives create a standalone defense, or apply MPC standards in a CL jurisdiction

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Doctrinal Framework Part I (necessity elements); justification-vs-excuse refinement; necessity-proportionality-standard refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Necessity, Ch 21, Refinement `necessity-proportionality-standard`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q1.** Your supervisor asks you to evaluate Foot's strongest defense for her continued operation of the shelter in violation of the condemnation order under the jurisdiction's applicable common law doctrine (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no reasonable legal alternative was available. <!-- correct -->
(b) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the common law formally exempts charitable actors from criminal liability whenever their actions are motivated by a sincere moral duty to assist vulnerable individuals during severe winter weather conditions.
(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the Model Penal Code, the harm avoided need only be greater than the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that threshold compared to the strict common law requirement of outweighing.
(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she is automatically deemed at fault for creating the emergency solely by operating a shelter in a building that was later condemned, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.
(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the mere existence of general building safety codes and condemnation procedures categorically preempts the necessity defense by foreclosing any judicial balancing of competing harms in this specific case.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Necessity is the correct defense here. Under the common law (Fact 6), a defendant must show: (1) the harm avoided clearly outweighs the harm caused, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) no reasonable legal alternative exists, and (4) the defendant was not at fault in creating the emergency. Option (a) correctly applies these elements. Option (b) is incorrect because the common law contains no blanket exemption for charitable actors or moral motives; the strict necessity elements must be met. Option (c) is incorrect because Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, not the MPC. Option (d) is incorrect because lawfully operating a shelter prior to its condemnation does not automatically constitute culpable fault in creating the weather emergency. Option (e) is incorrect because general building codes are not the type of particularized, specific legislative choice required to preempt the necessity defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, necessity-proportionality, justification-vs-excuse], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students assume moral motives create a standalone defense, or apply MPC standards in a CL jurisdiction

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Doctrinal Framework Part I (necessity elements); justification-vs-excuse refinement; necessity-proportionality-standard refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Option (a) accurately articulates the strict elements of the common law necessity defense and aligns perfectly with the facts. Under the common law, the defendant must face an imminent threat, have no reasonable legal alternative, and the harm avoided must clearly outweigh the harm caused. Saving twelve lives from freezing to death indisputably outweighs the regulatory harm of occupying a condemned building, making this a legally sound and factually supported application of the doctrine.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that under certain common law principles of justification, acting out of a moral duty to save lives constitutes a recognized exemption. If a jurisdiction strongly values charitable interventions, a student might infer that the common law has evolved to formally exempt sincere charitable actors responding to weather emergencies, making this the strongest policy-driven defense.
(c) Argument-for: A student might argue that (c) correctly identifies the nuanced difference between the Model Penal Code and the strict common law standard regarding proportionality. Even if the jurisdiction generally follows the common law, modern courts frequently look to the MPC's less stringent "greater than" test, and a student could believe that invoking this standard represents the strongest possible defense strategy.
(d) Argument-for: A strict reading of the common law necessity defense requires that the defendant be entirely free from fault in creating the emergency. A student could argue that by operating a shelter in a building that deteriorated to the point of condemnation, Foot was at fault for the residents having nowhere safe to go. Since she contributed to the predicament, the common law rigidly bars the necessity defense.
(e) Argument-for: The necessity defense is generally unavailable if the legislature has already weighed the competing values and made a specific choice. A student could argue that the enactment of building safety codes and the issuance of a condemnation order represent a categorical legislative preemption, meaning the state has already determined that the risk of occupation outweighs other concerns and foreclosed ad hoc judicial balancing.

Head-to-head: Option (a) correctly applies the common law elements of necessity to the facts of the emergency. Distractor (b) asserts a non-existent blanket exemption for charitable actors based on subjective moral duty. Distractor (c) explicitly relies on the Model Penal Code standard, directly contradicting the prompt's instruction to apply the "applicable common law doctrine." Distractor (d) makes the overly broad, falsifiable claim that lawfully operating a shelter automatically constitutes culpable fault for creating a subsequent weather emergency. Distractor (e) falsely claims that "general" building codes categorically preempt the necessity defense, whereas preemption requires a specific legislative choice addressing the emergency at hand. All distractors contain locked, falsifiable errors.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "formally exempts charitable actors from criminal liability whenever their actions are motivated by a sincere moral duty" — wrong because common law necessity relies on an objective balance of harms, not a categorical exemption for charitable motives.
- (c): "because under the Model Penal Code" — wrong because the prompt explicitly restricts the analysis to "the jurisdiction's applicable common law doctrine."
- (d): "automatically deemed at fault for creating the emergency solely by operating a shelter" — wrong because mere operation of a shelter does not automatically establish culpable fault in creating a weather emergency.
- (e): "the mere existence of general building safety codes... categorically preempts the necessity defense" — wrong because general safety regulations do not constitute the specific legislative preemption required to categorically bar necessity.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q1.** Your supervisor asks you to evaluate Foot's strongest defense for her continued operation of the shelter in violation of the condemnation order under the jurisdiction's applicable common law doctrine (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no reasonable legal alternative was available. <!-- correct -->
(b) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the common law formally exempts charitable actors from criminal liability whenever their actions are motivated by a sincere moral duty to assist vulnerable individuals during severe winter weather conditions.
(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the Model Penal Code, the harm avoided need only be greater than the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that threshold compared to the strict common law requirement of outweighing.
(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she is automatically deemed at fault for creating the emergency solely by operating a shelter in a building that was later condemned, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.
(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the mere existence of general building safety codes and condemnation procedures categorically preempts the necessity defense by foreclosing any judicial balancing of competing harms in this specific case.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Necessity is the correct defense here. Under the common law (Fact 6), a defendant must show: (1) the harm avoided clearly outweighs the harm caused, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) no reasonable legal alternative exists, and (4) the defendant was not at fault in creating the emergency. Option (a) correctly applies these elements. Option (b) is incorrect because the common law contains no blanket exemption for charitable actors or moral motives; the strict necessity elements must be met. Option (c) is incorrect because Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, not the MPC. Option (d) is incorrect because lawfully operating a shelter prior to its condemnation does not automatically constitute culpable fault in creating the weather emergency. Option (e) is incorrect because general building codes are not the type of particularized, specific legislative choice required to preempt the necessity defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, necessity-proportionality, justification-vs-excuse], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students assume moral motives create a standalone defense, or apply MPC standards in a CL jurisdiction

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Doctrinal Framework Part I (necessity elements); justification-vs-excuse refinement; necessity-proportionality-standard refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Foot perfectly satisfies the classic elements of the common law necessity defense. The threat of twelve people freezing to death in life-threatening cold constitutes an imminent harm. The harm caused—occupying a condemned building—is clearly outweighed by the preservation of human life. Assuming no reasonable legal alternatives were available, this option correctly and straightforwardly mirrors the strict common law requirements.
(b) Argument-for: A student might argue that the law of justification is fundamentally rooted in moral duty, and therefore common law jurisdictions functionally exempt charitable organizations from liability during extreme humanitarian crises. The argument would assert that a sincere moral motivation during severe winter weather overrides standard technical property offenses, creating a formal exemption for vulnerable-population assistance in the common law.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that Foot's strongest defense lies in the Model Penal Code's formulation of necessity, which only requires the harm avoided to be "greater than" the harm caused. By contrasting this with a stricter common law standard, the student might conclude that her only viable path is to persuade the court to adopt the MPC's more favorable threshold for preserving life.
(d) Argument-for: Common law necessity strictly bars the defense if the defendant is at fault for creating the emergency. A student might argue that Foot's decision to house individuals in a building with known structural issues leading to condemnation makes her inherently at fault. Therefore, the necessity defense is automatically precluded because she created the very conditions (people occupying a condemned building) that necessitated the violation.
(e) Argument-for: Necessity is a gap-filling doctrine that cannot override explicit legislative choices. A student could argue that the enactment of building safety codes and the issuance of a specific condemnation order represent a comprehensive legislative and administrative scheme. This scheme categorically preempts the necessity defense by foreclosing any ad hoc judicial balancing of harms.

Head-to-head: Option (a) accurately states and applies the common law elements of the necessity defense. The distractors all fail due to explicit, falsifiable legal claims. Option (b) invents a nonexistent blanket exemption for charitable actors. Option (c) relies on the Model Penal Code, directly contravening the prompt's instruction to apply the jurisdiction's common law doctrine. Option (d) incorrectly asserts that lawfully operating a shelter before condemnation constitutes automatic culpable fault in creating a subsequent weather emergency. Option (e) incorrectly states that general building codes categorically preempt the necessity defense; legislative preemption requires a much more specific, deliberate legislative choice of evils.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "the common law formally exempts charitable actors... whenever their actions are motivated by a sincere moral duty" — wrong because there is no such formal categorical exemption in common law necessity; the strict elements of the defense must still be satisfied.
- (c): "because under the Model Penal Code" — wrong because the prompt explicitly directs the student to apply "the jurisdiction's applicable common law doctrine."
- (d): "automatically deemed at fault... solely by operating a shelter in a building that was later condemned" — wrong because operating a building prior to its condemnation does not constitute automatic culpable fault in creating a weather-related necessity.
- (e): "mere existence of general building safety codes and condemnation procedures categorically preempts the necessity defense" — wrong because general codes do not categorically foreclose the necessity defense; legislative preemption requires a specific, deliberate choice of evils by the legislature that directly addresses the emergency at hand.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q02

### Grounding

**Q2.** Assume, hypothetically and without reference to the previous question, that Foot asserts a successful necessity defense against the condemnation violation charge. Your supervisor asks whether this defense impacts the potential liability of volunteers who helped Foot keep the shelter open (Facts 1 and 6). Under common law doctrine, which of the following best explains the relationship between Foot's defense and the volunteers' liability?

(a) The volunteers cannot share in Foot's defense because necessity is classified as a personal excuse under the common law, meaning each individual volunteer must independently establish their own excuse defense to avoid criminal liability.
(b) Because Foot's successful necessity defense renders her act legally justified rather than merely excused, the volunteers who assisted in that specific act share in the principal's justification and avoid criminal accomplice liability. <!-- correct -->
(c) The volunteers face criminal accomplice liability regardless of Foot's defense because the common law rule attaches upon purposeful assistance in prohibited conduct, completely precluding the transfer of any principal's legal defense.
(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each independently satisfies the elements of necessity, because derivative justification strictly requires every participating actor to independently prove they faced a strict choice of evils.
(e) The volunteers will automatically share in the defense because the common law treats necessity as a lesser-evils excuse, and all personal excuses freely transfer to accomplices who act with the exact same purpose as the main principal.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Necessity is a justification defense (a "choice of evils"), meaning the defendant's conduct is deemed legally permissible under the circumstances. Because justifications attach to the act itself rather than the actor, an accomplice who assists in a justified act commits no crime and derives the benefit of the justification. Option (b) correctly states this doctrine. Option (a) is incorrect because necessity is a justification, not a personal excuse. Option (c) is incorrect because accomplice liability requires a legally wrongful act by the principal; if the act is justified, there is no crime to aid. Option (d) is incorrect because accomplices to a justified act do not need to independently satisfy the primary justification's elements to avoid derivative liability. Option (e) is incorrect because necessity is a justification, not an excuse, and common law excuses do not automatically transfer to accomplices.

**Tags:** chapters: [18, 21], topics: [justification-vs-excuse, necessity, derivative-liability], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students classify necessity as an excuse and deny volunteers a derivative defense

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, justification-vs-excuse refinement; Chapter 18, dl-justification-vs-excuse.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Derivative Liability (Justification vs. Excuse), Ch 18 & 21, Refinements `dl-justification-vs-excuse` and `justification-vs-excuse`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q2.** Assume, hypothetically and without reference to the previous question, that Foot asserts a successful necessity defense against the condemnation violation charge. Your supervisor asks whether this defense impacts the potential liability of volunteers who helped Foot keep the shelter open (Facts 1 and 6). Under common law doctrine, which of the following best explains the relationship between Foot's defense and the volunteers' liability?

(a) The volunteers cannot share in Foot's defense because necessity is classified as a personal excuse under the common law, meaning each individual volunteer must independently establish their own excuse defense to avoid criminal liability.
(b) Because Foot's successful necessity defense renders her act legally justified rather than merely excused, the volunteers who assisted in that specific act share in the principal's justification and avoid criminal accomplice liability. <!-- correct -->
(c) The volunteers face criminal accomplice liability regardless of Foot's defense because the common law rule attaches upon purposeful assistance in prohibited conduct, completely precluding the transfer of any principal's legal defense.
(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each independently satisfies the elements of necessity, because derivative justification strictly requires every participating actor to independently prove they faced a strict choice of evils.
(e) The volunteers will automatically share in the defense because the common law treats necessity as a lesser-evils excuse, and all personal excuses freely transfer to accomplices who act with the exact same purpose as the main principal.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Necessity is a justification defense (a "choice of evils"), meaning the defendant's conduct is deemed legally permissible under the circumstances. Because justifications attach to the act itself rather than the actor, an accomplice who assists in a justified act commits no crime and derives the benefit of the justification. Option (b) correctly states this doctrine. Option (a) is incorrect because necessity is a justification, not a personal excuse. Option (c) is incorrect because accomplice liability requires a legally wrongful act by the principal; if the act is justified, there is no crime to aid. Option (d) is incorrect because accomplices to a justified act do not need to independently satisfy the primary justification's elements to avoid derivative liability. Option (e) is incorrect because necessity is a justification, not an excuse, and common law excuses do not automatically transfer to accomplices.

**Tags:** chapters: [18, 21], topics: [justification-vs-excuse, necessity, derivative-liability], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students classify necessity as an excuse and deny volunteers a derivative defense

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, justification-vs-excuse refinement; Chapter 18, dl-justification-vs-excuse.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that necessity conceptually functions as an excuse because the defendant acknowledges committing the actus reus but pleads external coercion via circumstances. Under the common law, excuses such as duress or insanity are strictly personal to the actor. If necessity is grouped with these personal excuses, it cannot transfer to accomplices. Therefore, option (a) provides a logical conclusion that each volunteer must independently prove their own excuse to escape liability.
(b) Argument-for: This option perfectly captures the common law doctrine of derivative liability and justification. Necessity is universally classified as a justification (a "choice of evils") because it renders the act itself legally permissible. Accomplice liability requires an underlying unlawful act by the principal. Because Foot's act is justified, there is no crime for the volunteers to have aided, meaning they automatically share in the defense.
(c) Argument-for: A student might argue that the common law focuses heavily on the accomplice's individual culpability and purposeful assistance in prohibited conduct. From this perspective, the volunteers committed the actus reus of aiding and possessed the mens rea of purpose. Option (c) asserts that accomplice liability attaches to this purposeful assistance regardless of the principal's defense, resting on the strict liability idea that an accomplice's fate is evaluated based on their independent wrongdoing rather than derivatively.
(d) Argument-for: A student could argue that even if necessity is a justification, the defense requires a highly specific set of circumstances (e.g., imminent harm, strict choice of evils) that must be assessed for each actor. Option (d) posits that justification does not flow automatically downward, but rather requires each participating actor to independently satisfy the elements of necessity. This reflects a strict reading of the necessity defense where derivative protection is rejected in favor of an individualized assessment of the choice of evils.
(e) Argument-for: A student might blur the lines between justification and excuse, arguing that necessity is a "lesser-evils excuse." If the volunteers shared Foot's exact purpose to prevent the greater evil, their culpability aligns perfectly with hers. Option (e) presents the appealing, albeit legally inaccurate, premise that common law allows excuses to freely transfer when accomplices share the exact same mental state and purpose as the principal.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is unambiguously correct because it accurately applies the fundamental common law rule that necessity is a justification, which attaches to the act and thus protects accomplices from derivative liability. Options (a) and (e) rely on the explicit misclassification of necessity as an excuse. Option (c) falsely claims that common law completely precludes the transfer of any legal defense to an accomplice. Option (d) falsely imposes an independent proof requirement on accomplices for derivative justifications. 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "necessity is classified as a personal excuse under the common law" — wrong because necessity is categorically classified as a justification under the common law.
- (c): "completely precluding the transfer of any principal's legal defense" — wrong because justification defenses attach to the act itself and absolutely transfer to accomplices, negating derivative liability.
- (d): "strictly requires every participating actor to independently prove they faced a strict choice of evils" — wrong because accomplices categorically share in the principal's justification without needing to independently satisfy the defense's elements.
- (e): "treats necessity as a lesser-evils excuse, and all personal excuses freely transfer" — wrong because necessity is a justification, and even if it were an excuse, personal excuses do not automatically transfer to accomplices.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q2.** Assume, hypothetically and without reference to the previous question, that Foot asserts a successful necessity defense against the condemnation violation charge. Your supervisor asks whether this defense impacts the potential liability of volunteers who helped Foot keep the shelter open (Facts 1 and 6). Under common law doctrine, which of the following best explains the relationship between Foot's defense and the volunteers' liability?

(a) The volunteers cannot share in Foot's defense because necessity is classified as a personal excuse under the common law, meaning each individual volunteer must independently establish their own excuse defense to avoid criminal liability.
(b) Because Foot's successful necessity defense renders her act legally justified rather than merely excused, the volunteers who assisted in that specific act share in the principal's justification and avoid criminal accomplice liability. <!-- correct -->
(c) The volunteers face criminal accomplice liability regardless of Foot's defense because the common law rule attaches upon purposeful assistance in prohibited conduct, completely precluding the transfer of any principal's legal defense.
(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each independently satisfies the elements of necessity, because derivative justification strictly requires every participating actor to independently prove they faced a strict choice of evils.
(e) The volunteers will automatically share in the defense because the common law treats necessity as a lesser-evils excuse, and all personal excuses freely transfer to accomplices who act with the exact same purpose as the main principal.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Necessity is a justification defense (a "choice of evils"), meaning the defendant's conduct is deemed legally permissible under the circumstances. Because justifications attach to the act itself rather than the actor, an accomplice who assists in a justified act commits no crime and derives the benefit of the justification. Option (b) correctly states this doctrine. Option (a) is incorrect because necessity is a justification, not a personal excuse. Option (c) is incorrect because accomplice liability requires a legally wrongful act by the principal; if the act is justified, there is no crime to aid. Option (d) is incorrect because accomplices to a justified act do not need to independently satisfy the primary justification's elements to avoid derivative liability. Option (e) is incorrect because necessity is a justification, not an excuse, and common law excuses do not automatically transfer to accomplices.

**Tags:** chapters: [18, 21], topics: [justification-vs-excuse, necessity, derivative-liability], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students classify necessity as an excuse and deny volunteers a derivative defense

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, justification-vs-excuse refinement; Chapter 18, dl-justification-vs-excuse.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that necessity is an excuse because it often responds to overwhelming pressure, akin to duress. If classified as a personal excuse, common law doctrine dictates that excuses are specific to the actor experiencing the condition. Therefore, volunteers wouldn't automatically benefit from Foot's defense and would need to independently establish their own excuse to avoid liability.
(b) Argument-for: Necessity is the classic "choice of evils" justification under the common law. A justified act is one that the law deems socially desirable or at least permissible under the specific circumstances. Because accomplice liability is derivative, if the principal's act is legally justified, no crime has occurred. Therefore, the accomplices cannot be liable for assisting a lawful act, and they share in the justification.
(c) Argument-for: A student might argue that under strict common law rules, accomplice liability attaches the moment one purposefully assists prohibited conduct. This perspective would assert that all legal defenses are intensely personal at common law and cannot shield third parties. Under this view, the volunteers' purposeful assistance in the prohibited act is enough to cement their liability, completely precluding any transfer of Foot's defense.
(d) Argument-for: A student could reason that while necessity is a justification, its application is contingent on the specific facts facing each actor. This argument posits that an accomplice cannot merely ride the coattails of the principal if they didn't personally face the imminent harm. Thus, to invoke the defense, derivative justification strictly requires that every participant independently satisfy the elements of a choice of evils.
(e) Argument-for: A student might argue that necessity is a "lesser-evils excuse" that reflects a concession to human frailty. Because the volunteers shared the exact same purpose as Foot in keeping the shelter open, the argument would suggest that their unified intent allows an excuse to freely transfer. Therefore, they would automatically share in the defense alongside the principal.

Head-to-head: Option (b) perfectly captures the common law distinction between justifications and excuses, correctly noting that necessity is a justification and that justified acts shield accomplices from derivative liability. The distractors fail by making explicit false claims about these doctrines. Options (a) and (e) misclassify necessity as an excuse. Option (c) falsely claims that common law completely precludes the transfer of any defense. Option (d) contradicts the very nature of derivative justification by requiring independent proof from accomplices.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "necessity is classified as a personal excuse under the common law" — wrong because necessity is unequivocally classified as a justification (a choice of evils) under standard common law doctrine.
- (c): "completely precluding the transfer of any principal's legal defense" — wrong because justification defenses, unlike excuses, attach to the act itself and thereby protect accomplices from derivative liability.
- (d): "derivative justification strictly requires every participating actor to independently prove they faced a strict choice of evils" — wrong because the doctrine of derivative justification shields accomplices based on the lawfulness of the principal's act, without requiring accomplices to independently satisfy the justification's elements.
- (e): "treats necessity as a lesser-evils excuse, and all personal excuses freely transfer" — wrong because necessity is a justification, not an excuse, and personal excuses specifically do not freely transfer to accomplices.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q03

### Grounding

**Q3.** Your supervisor turns to Holmes's liability for shoving Lisa Chen (Fact 3). Holmes claims self-defense. Under the common law standard of self-defense (as applied in *People v. Goetz*), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because he honestly believed Lisa was part of a forced-removal team, and an honest, purely subjective belief in the need for protective force is legally sufficient to establish self-defense under the common law, regardless of whether a reasonable person would share that assessment in those exact circumstances.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because a reasonable person in Holmes's situation—even after hearing Foot's warning about forced removal—would not have believed force was necessary to defend against an imminent use of unlawful force when faced with a small, unarmed woman identifying herself as a process server.

(c) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because Foot's earlier statement that "the city is going to send people to drag us out" constitutes prior threat information that automatically makes any subsequent belief in danger objectively reasonable, legally overriding the need to evaluate the peaceful actions of the actual individual he encountered.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim solely because he failed to retreat before using force. The common law imposes a strict duty to retreat prior to any use of defensive force, whether deadly or non-deadly, meaning he was legally required to exhaust all safe avenues of escape before standing his ground.

(e) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because his prior conviction for assault makes him a "person of violent character." Under the common law, anyone with a history of violent offenses is categorically barred from asserting an affirmative self-defense claim, regardless of the immediate circumstances they face.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Self-defense requires an objectively reasonable belief that force was necessary against an imminent use of unlawful force. The standard asks what a reasonable person in the defendant's situation would have believed (*People v. Goetz*). Holmes's "situation" legally incorporates his knowledge of Foot's prior warning. However, a reasonable person who hears a warning about forced removal, then encounters a small, unarmed woman who verbally identifies herself as a process server, would not conclude that an imminent use of unlawful force is underway. Therefore, his belief was objectively unreasonable. Option (a) is incorrect because the common law requires objective reasonableness, not merely an honest (purely subjective) belief. Option (c) is incorrect because while prior threat information is incorporated into the circumstances, it does not "automatically" make a subsequent belief objectively reasonable. Option (d) is incorrect because the common law duty to retreat applies only before using deadly force, and Holmes used non-deadly force. Option (e) is incorrect because a prior assault conviction does not categorically bar a defendant from raising a self-defense claim.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, imminence], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students apply a purely subjective standard or assume any prior experience makes the belief reasonable

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, objective-reasonableness-standard refinement; People v. Goetz.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — objective reasonableness standard and situational incorporation, Ch 22, Refinement `objective-reasonableness-standard` and `situational-incorporation`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q3.** Your supervisor turns to Holmes's liability for shoving Lisa Chen (Fact 3). Holmes claims self-defense. Under the common law standard of self-defense (as applied in *People v. Goetz*), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because he honestly believed Lisa was part of a forced-removal team, and an honest, purely subjective belief in the need for protective force is legally sufficient to establish self-defense under the common law, regardless of whether a reasonable person would share that assessment in those exact circumstances.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because a reasonable person in Holmes's situation—even after hearing Foot's warning about forced removal—would not have believed force was necessary to defend against an imminent use of unlawful force when faced with a small, unarmed woman identifying herself as a process server.

(c) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because Foot's earlier statement that "the city is going to send people to drag us out" constitutes prior threat information that automatically makes any subsequent belief in danger objectively reasonable, legally overriding the need to evaluate the peaceful actions of the actual individual he encountered.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim solely because he failed to retreat before using force. The common law imposes a strict duty to retreat prior to any use of defensive force, whether deadly or non-deadly, meaning he was legally required to exhaust all safe avenues of escape before standing his ground.

(e) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because his prior conviction for assault makes him a "person of violent character." Under the common law, anyone with a history of violent offenses is categorically barred from asserting an affirmative self-defense claim, regardless of the immediate circumstances they face.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Self-defense requires an objectively reasonable belief that force was necessary against an imminent use of unlawful force. The standard asks what a reasonable person in the defendant's situation would have believed (*People v. Goetz*). Holmes's "situation" legally incorporates his knowledge of Foot's prior warning. However, a reasonable person who hears a warning about forced removal, then encounters a small, unarmed woman who verbally identifies herself as a process server, would not conclude that an imminent use of unlawful force is underway. Therefore, his belief was objectively unreasonable. Option (a) is incorrect because the common law requires objective reasonableness, not merely an honest (purely subjective) belief. Option (c) is incorrect because while prior threat information is incorporated into the circumstances, it does not "automatically" make a subsequent belief objectively reasonable. Option (d) is incorrect because the common law duty to retreat applies only before using deadly force, and Holmes used non-deadly force. Option (e) is incorrect because a prior assault conviction does not categorically bar a defendant from raising a self-defense claim.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, imminence], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students apply a purely subjective standard or assume any prior experience makes the belief reasonable

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, objective-reasonableness-standard refinement; People v. Goetz.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might select this option by conflating the Model Penal Code's subjective approach to self-defense with the common law standard. Furthermore, because *People v. Goetz* famously emphasized the importance of evaluating the defendant's subjective situation, a student might mistakenly conclude that an honest subjective belief entirely satisfies the standard without an objective reasonableness requirement.
(b) Argument-for: This option correctly applies the bifurcated standard of *People v. Goetz*, which requires both a subjective belief and objective reasonableness. By incorporating Holmes's "situation" (the warning from Foot) but noting that an unarmed woman verbally identifying herself as a process server does not constitute an imminent threat of unlawful force, it correctly concludes the objective prong fails. Thus, Holmes lacks a valid self-defense claim.
(c) Argument-for: Because *Goetz* explicitly held that a defendant's prior experiences and knowledge must be considered, a student could argue that Foot's warning constitutes credible threat information. One might logically stretch this to argue that such highly specific threat information legally shifts the baseline of reasonableness, justifying Holmes's reaction to anyone approaching the property as part of a forced-removal team.
(d) Argument-for: A student aware of the common law "duty to retreat" rule in many jurisdictions might assume this duty applies across the board to all force. If one mistakenly believes the common law categorically prohibits any use of force if safe escape is possible, this option would seemingly provide a straightforward basis for denying Holmes's claim.
(e) Argument-for: A student might rely on the principle that an "initial aggressor" cannot claim self-defense and misapply character evidence rules. Under this flawed reasoning, a student could assume that an individual with a legally recognized violent history automatically forfeits the affirmative defense of self-defense, as their clean hands are compromised.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the clear correct answer because it correctly balances the subjective "situation" of the defendant with the objective reasonableness requirement articulated in *Goetz*. The distractors are effectively neutralized through the use of extreme, falsifiable absolutes. Distractor (a) falsely claims a "purely subjective belief" is "legally sufficient." Distractor (c) falsely asserts that prior threat information "automatically" makes subsequent beliefs reasonable. Distractor (d) contains the legally false claim that the duty to retreat applies strictly to "non-deadly" force. Distractor (e) includes the blatant misstatement that a violent history "categorically" bars self-defense. 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "an honest, purely subjective belief in the need for protective force is legally sufficient... regardless of whether a reasonable person would share that assessment" — wrong because *Goetz* and common law explicitly require the belief to be objectively reasonable.
- (c): "automatically makes any subsequent belief in danger objectively reasonable, legally overriding the need to evaluate..." — wrong because prior threat information is merely a factor in the totality of the circumstances, not an automatic legal override.
- (d): "strict duty to retreat prior to any use of defensive force, whether deadly or non-deadly" — wrong because the common law duty to retreat, where it exists, applies only to the use of deadly force.
- (e): "anyone with a history of violent offenses is categorically barred from asserting an affirmative self-defense claim" — wrong because past convictions do not legally forfeit a person's right to self-defense in subsequent, unrelated encounters.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q04

### Grounding

**Q4.** Returning to this jurisdiction's default rules (Fact 6), your supervisor asks about Holmes's claim that he stole the $5,000 under duress (Fact 4). Under the common law, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because Nietzsche threatened serious bodily injury to Holmes's mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's history, and the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline that places the threatened harm days in the future.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress requires a threat directed at the defendant personally, and the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members. Even though the threatened harm was aimed at his mother, the traditional rules strictly mandate that the defendant must be the direct target of the severe injury.

(c) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was not imminent -- Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, and the common law requires a present and immediate threat, not one that will materialize days later. Although the threat of severe harm targeted a close family member, the strict standard does not allow for a delay in the execution of the threatened physical injury.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law, which limits the defense to offenses against persons. While the threat to his mother involved serious bodily injury, the strict traditional rules bar the application of this affirmative excuse whenever the defendant commits a property offense, regardless of coercion.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense because a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific, and Nietzsche's history of violence makes the threat both credible and specific enough to satisfy the common law standard. Even though the harm was scheduled for a future date, his documented pattern of enforcing ultimatums dispenses with the need for immediate temporal proximity.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the common law (Fact 6), duress requires (1) a threat of death or serious bodily injury, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) the threat targets the defendant or a close relation, and (4) no reasonable legal alternative exists. Nietzsche's threat to "hurt her bad" satisfies the threat threshold and the target requirement (Holmes's mother is a close relation). However, the threat lacks imminence under the common law's strict standard. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday -- multiple days away. The common law requires a "present and immediate" threat, not one that will materialize days later. Option (a) falsely claims that the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats. This conflates the strict common-law rule with the *Dingwall* relaxation; under the common law, imminence requires a present and immediate threat, and a pattern of past violence does not substitute for temporal proximity. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, defeating the imminence element. Option (b) states that the common law "does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" -- the false proposition is this scope limitation. The common law extends duress to threats against the defendant or a close relation, and a mother is a close relation. Option (d) fabricates a crime-type limitation -- the false proposition is that "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes." The common law murder bar applies only to homicide; duress is available for property crimes. Option (e) states that "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" -- the false proposition is this restatement of the imminence rule. The common law requires imminence as a separate element; credibility and specificity do not substitute for temporal immediacy.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence, duress-threat-threshold, duress-target-scope], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and family relationship while overlooking that "until Friday" defeats CL imminence

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-threat-threshold refinement; duress-imminence-proximity refinement (CL requires imminent threat); Doctrinal Framework Part II.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duress, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-imminence-proximity`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q4.** Returning to this jurisdiction's default rules (Fact 6), your supervisor asks about Holmes's claim that he stole the $5,000 under duress (Fact 4). Under the common law, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because Nietzsche threatened serious bodily injury to Holmes's mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's history, and the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline that places the threatened harm days in the future.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress requires a threat directed at the defendant personally, and the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members. Even though the threatened harm was aimed at his mother, the traditional rules strictly mandate that the defendant must be the direct target of the severe injury.

(c) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was not imminent -- Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, and the common law requires a present and immediate threat, not one that will materialize days later. Although the threat of severe harm targeted a close family member, the strict standard does not allow for a delay in the execution of the threatened physical injury.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law, which limits the defense to offenses against persons. While the threat to his mother involved serious bodily injury, the strict traditional rules bar the application of this affirmative excuse whenever the defendant commits a property offense, regardless of coercion.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense because a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific, and Nietzsche's history of violence makes the threat both credible and specific enough to satisfy the common law standard. Even though the harm was scheduled for a future date, his documented pattern of enforcing ultimatums dispenses with the need for immediate temporal proximity.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the common law (Fact 6), duress requires (1) a threat of death or serious bodily injury, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) the threat targets the defendant or a close relation, and (4) no reasonable legal alternative exists. Nietzsche's threat to "hurt her bad" satisfies the threat threshold and the target requirement (Holmes's mother is a close relation). However, the threat lacks imminence under the common law's strict standard. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday -- multiple days away. The common law requires a "present and immediate" threat, not one that will materialize days later. Option (a) falsely claims that the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats. This conflates the strict common-law rule with the *Dingwall* relaxation; under the common law, imminence requires a present and immediate threat, and a pattern of past violence does not substitute for temporal proximity. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, defeating the imminence element. Option (b) states that the common law "does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" -- the false proposition is this scope limitation. The common law extends duress to threats against the defendant or a close relation, and a mother is a close relation. Option (d) fabricates a crime-type limitation -- the false proposition is that "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes." The common law murder bar applies only to homicide; duress is available for property crimes. Option (e) states that "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" -- the false proposition is this restatement of the imminence rule. The common law requires imminence as a separate element; credibility and specificity do not substitute for temporal immediacy.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence, duress-threat-threshold, duress-target-scope], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and family relationship while overlooking that "until Friday" defeats CL imminence

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-threat-threshold refinement; duress-imminence-proximity refinement (CL requires imminent threat); Doctrinal Framework Part II.

### Edge Case Audit

**Q4.** Returning to this jurisdiction's default rules (Fact 6), your supervisor asks about Holmes's claim that he stole the $5,000 under duress (Fact 4). Under the common law, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q4.** Returning to this jurisdiction's default rules (Fact 6), your supervisor asks about Holmes's claim that he stole the $5,000 under duress (Fact 4). Under the common law, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because Nietzsche threatened serious bodily injury to Holmes's mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's history, and the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline that places the threatened harm days in the future.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress requires a threat directed at the defendant personally, and the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members. Even though the threatened harm was aimed at his mother, the traditional rules strictly mandate that the defendant must be the direct target of the severe injury.

(c) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was not imminent -- Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, and the common law requires a present and immediate threat, not one that will materialize days later. Although the threat of severe harm targeted a close family member, the strict standard does not allow for a delay in the execution of the threatened physical injury.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law, which limits the defense to offenses against persons. While the threat to his mother involved serious bodily injury, the strict traditional rules bar the application of this affirmative excuse whenever the defendant commits a property offense, regardless of coercion.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense because a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific, and Nietzsche's history of violence makes the threat both credible and specific enough to satisfy the common law standard. Even though the harm was scheduled for a future date, his documented pattern of enforcing ultimatums dispenses with the need for immediate temporal proximity.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the common law (Fact 6), duress requires (1) a threat of death or serious bodily injury, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) the threat targets the defendant or a close relation, and (4) no reasonable legal alternative exists. Nietzsche's threat to "hurt her bad" satisfies the threat threshold and the target requirement (Holmes's mother is a close relation). However, the threat lacks imminence under the common law's strict standard. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday -- multiple days away. The common law requires a "present and immediate" threat, not one that will materialize days later. Option (a) falsely claims that the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats. This conflates the strict common-law rule with the *Dingwall* relaxation; under the common law, imminence requires a present and immediate threat, and a pattern of past violence does not substitute for temporal proximity. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, defeating the imminence element. Option (b) states that the common law "does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" -- the false proposition is this scope limitation. The common law extends duress to threats against the defendant or a close relation, and a mother is a close relation. Option (d) fabricates a crime-type limitation -- the false proposition is that "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes." The common law murder bar applies only to homicide; duress is available for property crimes. Option (e) states that "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" -- the false proposition is this restatement of the imminence rule. The common law requires imminence as a separate element; credibility and specificity do not substitute for temporal immediacy.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence, duress-threat-threshold, duress-target-scope], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and family relationship while overlooking that "until Friday" defeats CL imminence

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-threat-threshold refinement; duress-imminence-proximity refinement (CL requires imminent threat); Doctrinal Framework Part II.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under the common law, a credible and severe threat of injury to a close relative can sometimes blur the lines of traditional imminence if the threat is inescapable. A student might argue that when a threatening party has a documented history of enforcing ultimatums, the inevitability of the harm transforms the future deadline into a functionally immediate threat. Option (a) appeals to this logic by explicitly stating that a documented pattern satisfies the imminence requirement, validating the defense.

(b) Argument-for: A student might argue that the strictest, most traditional version of the common law limits the duress defense to threats against the defendant alone. Because duress requires an immediate, overpowering physical threat that breaks the defendant's will, the law historically required the defendant to be the direct target of the physical harm. Option (b) captures this restrictive view, asserting that threats to third parties, even close family members, categorically cannot support the defense.

(c) Argument-for: Under the common law, the duress defense strictly requires a "present, imminent, and impending" threat of death or serious bodily injury. The threat here was conditioned on a future deadline ("until Friday"), meaning it was not immediate and Holmes had time to seek legal alternatives, such as contacting the police. While the threat targeted a close relative (his mother) and involved severe injury, the lack of strict temporal imminence is fatal. Option (c) correctly concludes that the delay in execution defeats the imminence requirement.

(d) Argument-for: A student might recall that duress is subject to significant categorical limitations under the common law, most notably the bar against using it for intentional homicide. One might erroneously extend this logic to argue that property offenses also lack the necessary physical harm to justify submitting to coercion. Option (d) presents a bright-line rule that duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes, suggesting the common law strictly limits the defense to offenses against persons.

(e) Argument-for: While the common law generally mentions imminence, a student might argue that the core purpose of the imminence requirement is simply to ensure the threat is genuine and unavoidable. If a threat is highly credible and specific—bolstered by a history of violence—the strict temporal requirement is conceptually satisfied. Option (e) relies on this functional interpretation, asserting that Nietzsche's documented pattern of violence dispenses with the need for immediate temporal proximity.

Head-to-head: 
Option (c) accurately applies the common law's strict imminence element, identifying that a threat to be carried out "until Friday" is not present and immediate. The distractors all rely on explicit misstatements of common law doctrine. Option (a) wrongly claims that a documented pattern mathematically satisfies imminence regardless of deadlines. Option (b) falsely asserts that the common law categorically excludes threats to close family members. Option (d) fabricates a categorical bar on duress for theft crimes, misapplying the homicide exception. Option (e) explicitly, and incorrectly, states that a threat need not be imminent under the common law if it is credible and specific. Since each distractor hinges on a locked, objectively false claim, the question is highly defensible.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline" — wrong because the common law strictly requires temporal immediacy; a pattern of past violence does not override a multi-day deadline.
- (b): "the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" — wrong because the common law explicitly allows the defense for threats directed at the defendant or a close relation (like a mother).
- (d): "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law" — wrong because the absolute categorical bar applies only to intentional homicide, not to theft or other property offenses.
- (e): "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" — wrong because the common law strictly requires imminence as a distinct, mandatory element that cannot be waived by credibility or specificity.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q05

### Grounding

**Q5.** Assume, counterfactually, that this case arose in a jurisdiction that applies the *Dingwall* exception, which relaxes the traditional strict physical imminence requirement for duress based on the psychological effects of systemic coercion. Assume Nietzsche had explicitly commanded Holmes to steal the money, had threatened Holmes for months, and Holmes reasonably believed police were unable to protect his mother. Under this framework, can Holmes satisfy the imminence requirement? (Fact 4, modified.)

(a) Yes, because the exception establishes that any pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats creates a reasonable perception of imminent danger under the law, regardless of the prior relationship between the coercer and the defendant.
(b) No, because the exception that relaxes the strict physical imminence requirement is specifically tied to the dynamics of battering and intimate coercive control, which do not apply to a generalized extortion dispute between former business associates. <!-- correct -->
(c) Yes, because the exception eliminates the imminence requirement entirely in such cases, allowing any credible threat of future harm to satisfy the defense if the threatening party has a documented track record of severe violence against the defendant.
(d) No, because the exception strictly applies only when the defendant is physically confined, completely isolated, or actively restrained by the threatening party at the exact moment the underlying criminal offense is being committed by the defendant.
(e) No, because the exception is strictly limited to cases where the threatened physical harm is directed at the defendant personally, and it cannot be invoked based on generalized threats made against a family member or other third-party relation.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under *United States v. Dingwall*, the strict physical proximity and imminence requirements of duress are relaxed for defendants experiencing the effects of battering or coercive control. The specific psychological dynamics of battering can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger even when the coercer is physically absent. However, courts do not extend this exception to generalized criminal extortion or associate disputes, as doing so would swallow the traditional strict imminence rule. Thus, (b) is correct. Option (a) falsely broadens the doctrine into a blanket exception for any sustained threats. Option (c) falsely asserts the rule eliminates the imminence requirement entirely. Option (d) falsely claims physical confinement is required; *Dingwall* expressly held the batterer's physical presence or confinement is not necessary. Option (e) falsely states that the exception cannot be based on threats to third parties; traditional duress principles covering threats to close relatives still apply.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence-proximity, duress-corrupt-authorities], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students incorrectly apply Dingwall's relaxed imminence to general extortion cases rather than limiting it to battering/coercive control

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duress imminence exception (Dingwall), Ch 21, Refinement `duress-imminence-proximity`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Edge Case Audit

**Q5.** Assume, counterfactually, that this case arose in a jurisdiction that applies the *Dingwall* exception, which relaxes the traditional strict physical imminence requirement for duress based on the psychological effects of systemic coercion. Assume Nietzsche had explicitly commanded Holmes to steal the money, had threatened Holmes for months, and Holmes reasonably believed police were unable to protect his mother. Under this framework, can Holmes satisfy the imminence requirement? (Fact 4, modified.)

(a) Yes, because the exception establishes that any pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats creates a reasonable perception of imminent danger under the law, regardless of the prior relationship between the coercer and the defendant.
(b) No, because the exception that relaxes the strict physical imminence requirement is specifically tied to the dynamics of battering and intimate coercive control, which do not apply to a generalized extortion dispute between former business associates. <!-- correct -->
(c) Yes, because the exception eliminates the imminence requirement entirely in such cases, allowing any credible threat of future harm to satisfy the defense if the threatening party has a documented track record of severe violence against the defendant.
(d) No, because the exception strictly applies only when the defendant is physically confined, completely isolated, or actively restrained by the threatening party at the exact moment the underlying criminal offense is being committed by the defendant.
(e) No, because the exception is strictly limited to cases where the threatened physical harm is directed at the defendant personally, and it cannot be invoked based on generalized threats made against a family member or other third-party relation.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under *United States v. Dingwall*, the strict physical proximity and imminence requirements of duress are relaxed for defendants experiencing the effects of battering or coercive control. The specific psychological dynamics of battering can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger even when the coercer is physically absent. However, courts do not extend this exception to generalized criminal extortion or associate disputes, as doing so would swallow the traditional strict imminence rule. Thus, (b) is correct. Option (a) falsely broadens the doctrine into a blanket exception for any sustained threats. Option (c) falsely asserts the rule eliminates the imminence requirement entirely. Option (d) falsely claims physical confinement is required; *Dingwall* expressly held the batterer's physical presence or confinement is not necessary. Option (e) falsely states that the exception cannot be based on threats to third parties; traditional duress principles covering threats to close relatives still apply.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence-proximity, duress-corrupt-authorities], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students incorrectly apply Dingwall's relaxed imminence to general extortion cases rather than limiting it to battering/coercive control

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q5.** Assume, counterfactually, that this case arose in a jurisdiction that applies the *Dingwall* exception, which relaxes the traditional strict physical imminence requirement for duress based on the psychological effects of systemic coercion. Assume Nietzsche had explicitly commanded Holmes to steal the money, had threatened Holmes for months, and Holmes reasonably believed police were unable to protect his mother. Under this framework, can Holmes satisfy the imminence requirement? (Fact 4, modified.)

(a) Yes, because the exception establishes that any pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats creates a reasonable perception of imminent danger under the law, regardless of the prior relationship between the coercer and the defendant.
(b) No, because the exception that relaxes the strict physical imminence requirement is specifically tied to the dynamics of battering and intimate coercive control, which do not apply to a generalized extortion dispute between former business associates. <!-- correct -->
(c) Yes, because the exception eliminates the imminence requirement entirely in such cases, allowing any credible threat of future harm to satisfy the defense if the threatening party has a documented track record of severe violence against the defendant.
(d) No, because the exception strictly applies only when the defendant is physically confined, completely isolated, or actively restrained by the threatening party at the exact moment the underlying criminal offense is being committed by the defendant.
(e) No, because the exception is strictly limited to cases where the threatened physical harm is directed at the defendant personally, and it cannot be invoked based on generalized threats made against a family member or other third-party relation.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under *United States v. Dingwall*, the strict physical proximity and imminence requirements of duress are relaxed for defendants experiencing the effects of battering or coercive control. The specific psychological dynamics of battering can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger even when the coercer is physically absent. However, courts do not extend this exception to generalized criminal extortion or associate disputes, as doing so would swallow the traditional strict imminence rule. Thus, (b) is correct. Option (a) falsely broadens the doctrine into a blanket exception for any sustained threats. Option (c) falsely asserts the rule eliminates the imminence requirement entirely. Option (d) falsely claims physical confinement is required; *Dingwall* expressly held the batterer's physical presence or confinement is not necessary. Option (e) falsely states that the exception cannot be based on threats to third parties; traditional duress principles covering threats to close relatives still apply.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence-proximity, duress-corrupt-authorities], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students incorrectly apply Dingwall's relaxed imminence to general extortion cases rather than limiting it to battering/coercive control

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: If the jurisdiction has adopted the *Dingwall* exception focusing on the "psychological effects of systemic coercion," a student could argue that this rationale applies equitably to any relationship where systemic coercion exists. The underlying logic of the exception is that sustained trauma alters the defendant's reasonable perception of inescapable danger. Therefore, any continuous pattern of unpredictable threats that successfully instills this psychological effect should legally suffice to relax the imminence requirement, regardless of whether the coercer is an intimate partner or a business associate.
(b) Argument-for: *United States v. Dingwall* created a narrow exception to the strict physical imminence requirement specifically tailored to the psychological realities of battering and intimate partner coercive control. Courts are highly protective of the traditional imminence rule and have explicitly refused to extend the *Dingwall* rationale to general criminal extortion or gang disputes, fearing it would swallow the rule. Because the Holmes-Nietzsche dynamic is an extortion dispute between associates, the battering exception does not apply, and Holmes cannot satisfy the strict imminence requirement.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that by recognizing the "psychological effects of systemic coercion," the *Dingwall* exception functionally eliminates the imminence requirement as traditionally defined. If systemic threats and a documented track record of severe violence are enough to trigger the defense even when the coercer is absent, the concept of temporal "imminence" is effectively replaced by a broader inquiry into the credibility and inescapability of future harm. Thus, in cases of severe prolonged coercion, the imminence requirement is essentially discarded.
(d) Argument-for: A student might reason that even under a relaxed standard, duress requires an explanation for why the defendant could not safely seek police assistance. They could argue that the *Dingwall* exception only relaxes the coercer's physical presence if the defendant is otherwise physically isolated or confined by the coercer's network or resources. This creates a logical rule that the exception applies only when the defendant is actively restrained or physically completely isolated at the moment of the offense.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that the psychological evidence underlying the *Dingwall* exception (such as Battered Woman Syndrome) relates uniquely to the traumatized victim's hyper-vigilance regarding threats to their own bodily integrity. While traditional duress allows for threats to third parties, a student might reason that the specialized psychological relaxation of imminence cannot be automatically transferred to threats against a family member, making the exception strictly limited to self-directed threats.

Head-to-head: Option (b) correctly applies the specific, doctrinally bounded holding of *Dingwall*, which strictly anchors the relaxed imminence standard to the dynamics of battering and intimate coercive control rather than general extortion. All distractors are cleanly falsifiable due to their absolute language. Option (a) falsely claims the exception applies to "any pattern" "regardless of the prior relationship." Option (c) falsely claims the exception "eliminates the imminence requirement entirely." Option (d) imposes a false categorical requirement of physical confinement "at the exact moment." Option (e) imposes a false categorical ban on applying the defense to threats against family members.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "regardless of the prior relationship between the coercer and the defendant" — wrong because *Dingwall* specifically relies on the dynamics of battering/intimate partner violence and courts actively refuse to apply it to general extortion.
- (c): "eliminates the imminence requirement entirely" — wrong because *Dingwall* modifies or relaxes the imminence inquiry (assessing it through the lens of the psychological effects of battering) rather than eliminating the imminence element entirely.
- (d): "strictly applies only when the defendant is physically confined... at the exact moment" — wrong because *Dingwall* explicitly allows the defense even when the defendant is not physically confined or in the immediate physical presence of the coercer.
- (e): "strictly limited to cases where the threatened physical harm is directed at the defendant personally" — wrong because traditional duress extends to threats against close family members, and the *Dingwall* exception does not categorically abrogate this foundational rule.

Recommended fix: none
-->

(a) Yes, because the exception establishes that any pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats creates a reasonable perception of imminent danger under the law, regardless of the prior relationship between the coercer and the defendant.
(b) No, because the exception that relaxes the strict physical imminence requirement is specifically tied to the dynamics of battering and intimate coercive control, which do not apply to a generalized extortion dispute between former business associates. <!-- correct -->

(e) No, because the exception is strictly limited to cases where the threatened physical harm is directed at the defendant personally, and it cannot be invoked based on generalized threats made against a family member or other third-party relation.
