## Q06

### Grounding

**Q6.** Your supervisor asks you to analyze an assault charge against Markov for attacking Wald, a city inspector. Markov acted under a schizophrenic delusion that Wald was an assassin sent to poison his shelter, genuinely believing his attack was morally justified to save lives. Markov later told police, "I was stopping an assassination." A psychiatrist will testify that while Markov understood he was physically striking a person, his acute psychotic episode prevented him from appreciating that his actions were wrong. Under the Model Penal Code (MPC) insanity framework, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Markov's defense?

(a) Markov has a strong insanity defense because he did not know the physical nature and quality of his act—he thought he was stopping an assassination, meaning he did not understand he was striking a human being. This pure cognitive failure alone satisfies the MPC's threshold requirement for full acquittal at trial.
(b) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because his post-assault statement to police—"I was stopping an assassination"—demonstrates that he understood the physical mechanics of his actions, which automatically equates to appreciating wrongfulness and precludes lack of substantial capacity under the MPC.
(c) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because paranoid schizophrenia is legally defined as an abnormality "manifested only by repeated criminal or otherwise antisocial conduct" and is therefore categorically excluded from qualifying as a mental disease under the explicit limiting provisions of the MPC.
(d) Markov has a strong insanity defense because, as a result of his schizophrenia (a qualifying mental disease), he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct—he understood he was physically striking a human being but genuinely believed the act was morally justified to save lives. <!-- correct -->
(e) Markov has a strong insanity defense because his schizophrenia entirely destroyed his capacity to control his physical behavior, satisfying the strict irresistible impulse test—he could not have stopped his attack, which represents the exact legal standard required to satisfy the MPC's volitional impairment rule.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC § 4.01, a defendant is not responsible if, due to a mental disease or defect, he lacks "substantial capacity" to "appreciate" the wrongfulness of his conduct. Markov suffers from a qualifying mental disease (schizophrenia) and lacked substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness because his delusion led him to believe he was saving lives. (a) is incorrect because Markov understood the physical nature of his act (he knew he was striking a person). (b) is incorrect because understanding the physical mechanics of an act does not automatically equate to appreciating its wrongfulness. (c) is incorrect because schizophrenia is a paradigmatic mental disease; the MPC exclusion applies instead to psychopathy or antisocial personality disorder. (e) is incorrect because the MPC uses "lacks substantial capacity" rather than requiring complete destruction of control, and the facts establish a cognitive, rather than volitional, impairment.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mpc-appreciate-criminality, mnaghten-wrongness, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse the nature-and-quality prong with the wrongfulness prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mnaghten-wrongness refinement; mpc-appreciate-criminality refinement; qualifying-mental-disease refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — MPC Insanity Defense, Ch 23, Refinement `mpc-appreciate-criminality`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

(a) Argument-for: Under a pure cognitive test (which influences the MPC's appreciation prong), a profound delusion about the context of the act could be framed as a failure to understand its nature and quality. Under this theory, because his schizophrenia manifested in an assault, it is categorically excluded by the MPC's limiting provisions.

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q6.** Your supervisor asks you to analyze an assault charge against Markov for attacking Wald, a city inspector. Markov acted under a schizophrenic delusion that Wald was an assassin sent to poison his shelter, genuinely believing his attack was morally justified to save lives. Markov later told police, "I was stopping an assassination." A psychiatrist will testify that while Markov understood he was physically striking a person, his acute psychotic episode prevented him from appreciating that his actions were wrong. Under the Model Penal Code (MPC) insanity framework, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Markov's defense?

(a) Markov has a strong insanity defense because he did not know the physical nature and quality of his act—he thought he was stopping an assassination, meaning he did not understand he was striking a human being. This pure cognitive failure alone satisfies the MPC's threshold requirement for full acquittal at trial.
(b) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because his post-assault statement to police—"I was stopping an assassination"—demonstrates that he understood the physical mechanics of his actions, which automatically equates to appreciating wrongfulness and precludes lack of substantial capacity under the MPC.
(c) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because paranoid schizophrenia is legally defined as an abnormality "manifested only by repeated criminal or otherwise antisocial conduct" and is therefore categorically excluded from qualifying as a mental disease under the explicit limiting provisions of the MPC.
(d) Markov has a strong insanity defense because, as a result of his schizophrenia (a qualifying mental disease), he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct—he understood he was physically striking a human being but genuinely believed the act was morally justified to save lives. <!-- correct -->
(e) Markov has a strong insanity defense because his schizophrenia entirely destroyed his capacity to control his physical behavior, satisfying the strict irresistible impulse test—he could not have stopped his attack, which represents the exact legal standard required to satisfy the MPC's volitional impairment rule.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC § 4.01, a defendant is not responsible if, due to a mental disease or defect, he lacks "substantial capacity" to "appreciate" the wrongfulness of his conduct. Markov suffers from a qualifying mental disease (schizophrenia) and lacked substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness because his delusion led him to believe he was saving lives. (a) is incorrect because Markov understood the physical nature of his act (he knew he was striking a person). (b) is incorrect because understanding the physical mechanics of an act does not automatically equate to appreciating its wrongfulness. (c) is incorrect because schizophrenia is a paradigmatic mental disease; the MPC exclusion applies instead to psychopathy or antisocial personality disorder. (e) is incorrect because the MPC uses "lacks substantial capacity" rather than requiring complete destruction of control, and the facts establish a cognitive, rather than volitional, impairment.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mpc-appreciate-criminality, mnaghten-wrongness, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse the nature-and-quality prong with the wrongfulness prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mnaghten-wrongness refinement; mpc-appreciate-criminality refinement; qualifying-mental-disease refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that believing one is attacking an assassin rather than an inspector fundamentally changes the "nature and quality" of the act. If Markov's delusion was so severe that he perceived his victim as a lethal threat rather than an innocent human official, his cognitive impairment was paramount. The student might conclude this severe misapprehension of the situation means he essentially did not understand the true nature of his physical actions.
(b) Argument-for: A student might argue that under the MPC, verbalizing a coherent goal like "stopping an assassination" proves the defendant possessed significant cognitive clarity and rationality. Because he understood the physical mechanics of his attack, a student might deduce that he must have known he was committing a violent act. From this, one could mistakenly infer that understanding physical violence categorically precludes finding a lack of substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness.
(c) Argument-for: The MPC contains an explicit carve-out stating that "mental disease or defect" does not include an abnormality manifested only by repeated criminal or antisocial conduct. A student might view Markov's unprovoked assault on an inspector as a clear example of "antisocial conduct." If the student believes schizophrenia primarily manifests through such erratic or violent behavior in the criminal context, they could mistakenly apply this exclusionary rule to his diagnosis.
(d) Argument-for: This is the correct application of the MPC standard. Under MPC § 4.01, a defendant is not responsible if, due to a mental disease or defect, they lack "substantial capacity" to "appreciate the criminality [wrongfulness]" of their conduct. Markov's schizophrenia qualifies as a mental disease, and his delusion that he was morally justified to save lives directly impaired his capacity to appreciate that his assault was wrong, perfectly satisfying the cognitive prong of the MPC test.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that Markov's delusion imposed an overwhelming compulsion to act to save lives, effectively destroying his volition. Since the MPC includes a volitional prong (ability to conform conduct to the law), a student might conflate this with the irresistible impulse test. By arguing that the delusion compelled him, the student could conclude that the total destruction of his physical control satisfies the MPC's requirement for volitional impairment.

Head-to-head: Option (d) flawlessly applies the MPC insanity test to the facts, recognizing that Markov's delusion impaired his appreciation of wrongfulness despite his understanding of the physical act. Option (a) contradicts the fact pattern, as the prompt explicitly states Markov knew he was striking a person. Option (b) fails legally because understanding physical mechanics does not automatically equate to appreciating wrongfulness under the MPC. Option (c) erroneously applies the MPC's psychopathy exclusion (manifested only by antisocial conduct) to paranoid schizophrenia. Option (e) uses incorrect legal standards by insisting the MPC requires "entirely destroyed" capacity and relies on the "strict irresistible impulse test," whereas the MPC specifically uses the relaxed "lacks substantial capacity" standard. All distractors are reliably falsifiable.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "meaning he did not understand he was striking a human being" — wrong because it explicitly contradicts the prompt's factual premise that "Markov understood he was physically striking a person."
- (b): "which automatically equates to appreciating wrongfulness" — wrong because the MPC explicitly bifurcates the understanding of physical mechanics from the appreciation of wrongfulness or criminality; they are not legally equivalent.
- (c): "paranoid schizophrenia is legally defined as an abnormality 'manifested only by repeated criminal or otherwise antisocial conduct'" — wrong because this is the statutory definition used by the MPC to exclude psychopathy and antisocial personality disorder, not paranoid schizophrenia.
- (e): "entirely destroyed his capacity" and "strict irresistible impulse test... represents the exact legal standard required" — wrong because the MPC explicitly requires only a lack of "substantial capacity" and was drafted specifically to reject the total-deprivation requirement of the strict irresistible impulse test.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q07

### Grounding

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (IDRA) standard (provided in the statutes) rather than the provided Model Penal Code (MPC) standard. Your supervisor asks how applying the IDRA rules instead of the MPC rules would change the analysis of Markov's attack on Wald (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests—both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct. Given Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia and acute psychotic episode, he clearly satisfies this "substantial capacity" threshold under either framework.
(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act. Since Markov understood the physical nature of his actions—that he was striking a person—his delusions about justification would be irrelevant under this approach.
(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant. Therefore, if the jury harbored any reasonable doubt about whether Markov's schizophrenia impaired his cognition during the offense, they would be mandated to acquit him.
(d) The IDRA standard makes the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires the disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, changes "substantial capacity" to "unable to appreciate," and demands proof by clear and convincing evidence. However, Markov's severe schizophrenia causing an inability to appreciate wrongfulness satisfies even this strict threshold. <!-- correct -->
(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total physical incapacitation. Because Markov was not physically incapacitated, his delusions would be deemed legally insufficient under this federal rule.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** The provided IDRA statute differs from the MPC by requiring a "severe" mental disease, stating the defendant must be "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness (rather than lacking "substantial capacity"), omitting the volitional prong, and placing the burden on the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. Markov's defense remains strong: paranoid schizophrenia is a severe disease, and his delusion shows he was unable to appreciate wrongfulness. Option (a) is incorrect because IDRA uses the stricter "unable" standard, not "substantial capacity." Option (b) is incorrect because the IDRA text explicitly retains the wrongfulness prong. Option (c) is incorrect because IDRA shifts the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence, contradicting the claim that the prosecution must disprove it. Option (e) is incorrect because the statute does not require physical incapacitation to qualify as severe.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, mpc-appreciate-criminality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students conflate IDRA and MPC because both have a cognitive prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, federal-idra-requirements refinement; burden-allocation-post-hinckley refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — federal IDRA requirements, Ch 23, Refinement `federal-idra-requirements` (and `burden-allocation-post-hinckley`). Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (IDRA) standard (provided in the statutes) rather than the provided Model Penal Code (MPC) standard. Your supervisor asks how applying the IDRA rules instead of the MPC rules would change the analysis of Markov's attack on Wald (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests—both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct. Given Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia and acute psychotic episode, he clearly satisfies this "substantial capacity" threshold under either framework.
(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act. Since Markov understood the physical nature of his actions—that he was striking a person—his delusions about justification would be irrelevant under this approach.
(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant. Therefore, if the jury harbored any reasonable doubt about whether Markov's schizophrenia impaired his cognition during the offense, they would be mandated to acquit him.
(d) The IDRA standard makes the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires the disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, changes "substantial capacity" to "unable to appreciate," and demands proof by clear and convincing evidence. However, Markov's severe schizophrenia causing an inability to appreciate wrongfulness satisfies even this strict threshold. <!-- correct -->
(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total physical incapacitation. **Grounding:** Chapter 23, federal-idra-requirements refinement; burden-allocation-post-hinckley refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that in application, courts treat the cognitive prongs of the MPC and IDRA as functionally synonymous when dealing with profound psychosis. If a defendant is deeply delusional, the distinction between "lacks substantial capacity" and "unable" collapses, making the statutory difference essentially negligible in practice. Therefore, option (a) could be viewed as the most practically accurate comparison for a case of acute schizophrenia.
(b) Argument-for: Historically, some strict interpretations of cognitive insanity tests focused solely on the physical nature of the act. A student could mistakenly believe the post-Hinckley IDRA went even further than M'Naghten by entirely striking the wrongfulness prong to aggressively curtail acquittals based on moral delusions. Under this reasoning, Markov's understanding of his physical motions would indeed defeat his defense.
(c) Argument-for: Prior to the IDRA, the federal rule required the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt (as famously seen in the Hinckley trial). A student who confuses the pre-Hinckley federal common law with the post-Hinckley IDRA statute might assert that the federal system is structurally more favorable to defendants regarding the burden of proof. Thus, option (c) correctly states the old federal baseline.
(d) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies every statutory change enacted by the IDRA: the addition of "severe," the elimination of the volitional prong, the shift from "substantial capacity" to "unable" (a stricter cognitive threshold), and the shift of the burden of proof to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. It correctly concludes that a profoundly psychotic defendant like Markov could still meet this higher bar.
(e) Argument-for: A student might seize on the word "severe" and assume it imposes a rigid, physical requirement to categorically exclude personality disorders and less profound mental illnesses. They could theorize that federal courts interpreted "severe" to require total physical incapacitation or automatism, thereby making Markov's merely cognitive/delusional impairment insufficient.

Head-to-head: Option (d) perfectly captures the text of the IDRA (18 U.S.C § 17) and correctly applies it to the facts. The distractors all rely on explicitly false statements of black-letter federal law. Option (a) falsely claims the IDRA uses "substantial capacity." Option (b) falsely claims the IDRA eliminated the wrongfulness prong. Option (c) falsely claims the IDRA places the burden of proof on the prosecution beyond a reasonable doubt. Option (e) uses an absolute ("solely") and introduces a fabricated legal rule ("total physical incapacitation") to define "severe." Since every distractor features a clear, objectively falsifiable error, the question is structurally sound and robust.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests—both ask whether the defendant 'lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness'" — wrong because the IDRA explicitly uses the stricter "unable to appreciate" language, not "substantial capacity."
- (b): "IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong" — wrong because 18 U.S.C. § 17 specifically requires the defendant be unable to appreciate the nature and quality "or the wrongfulness" of his acts.
- (c): "IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt" — wrong because the IDRA affirmatively shifted the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence.
- (e): "solely because IDRA adds... which excludes psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total physical incapacitation" — wrong because "severe" does not require physical incapacitation, and "solely" ignores the other massive changes to the statute (e.g., burden of proof, volitional prong).

Recommended fix: none

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (IDRA) standard (provided in the statutes) rather than the provided Model Penal Code (MPC) standard. Your supervisor asks how applying the IDRA rules instead of the MPC rules would change the analysis of Markov's attack on Wald (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests—both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct. Given Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia and acute psychotic episode, he clearly satisfies this "substantial capacity" threshold under either framework.
(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act. Since Markov understood the physical nature of his actions—that he was striking a person—his delusions about justification would be irrelevant under this approach.
(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant. Therefore, if the jury harbored any reasonable doubt about whether Markov's schizophrenia impaired his cognition during the offense, they would be mandated to acquit him.
(d) The IDRA standard makes the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires the disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, changes "substantial capacity" to "unable to appreciate," and demands proof by clear and convincing evidence. However, Markov's severe schizophrenia causing an inability to appreciate wrongfulness satisfies even this strict threshold. <!-- correct -->
(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total physical incapacitation. Because Markov was not physically incapacitated, his delusions would be deemed legally insufficient under this federal rule.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** The provided IDRA statute differs from the MPC by requiring a "severe" mental disease, stating the defendant must be "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness (rather than lacking "substantial capacity"), omitting the volitional prong, and placing the burden on the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. Markov's defense remains strong: paranoid schizophrenia is a severe disease, and his delusion shows he was unable to appreciate wrongfulness. Option (a) is incorrect because IDRA uses the stricter "unable" standard, not "substantial capacity." Option (b) is incorrect because the IDRA text explicitly retains the wrongfulness prong. Option (c) is incorrect because IDRA shifts the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence, contradicting the claim that the prosecution must disprove it. Option (e) is incorrect because the statute does not require physical incapacitation to qualify as severe.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, mpc-appreciate-criminality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students conflate IDRA and MPC because both have a cognitive prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, federal-idra-requirements refinement; burden-allocation-post-hinckley refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that the cognitive prongs of the MPC and IDRA are functionally synonymous, as both target the defendant's ability to grasp the wrongfulness of their conduct. Because courts often interpret "substantial capacity" and "unable" as targeting the same cognitive deficits in severe schizophrenia cases, a student could mistakenly conclude the legal texts share the exact same phrase. Under this view, Markov's acute psychosis easily clears the threshold under both tests, meaning the shift in rules makes no practical difference.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that IDRA represents a return to a strict M'Naghten-style test, stripping away the broader moral "wrongfulness" inquiries favored by the MPC. If IDRA only asks whether the defendant knew they were striking a person, Markov's understanding of the physical reality of his attack would categorically defeat his defense. This argument relies on interpreting the IDRA's conservative backlash against Hinckley as entirely eliminating the subjective wrongfulness prong.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that due process requires the prosecution to prove all elements of a crime, including the requisite mental state, beyond a reasonable doubt. Historically, federal courts did require the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt once raised (the pre-Hinckley federal rule). Therefore, a student might mistakenly believe the IDRA codified this historical federal standard, making it highly advantageous for the defendant.
(d) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the exact statutory changes enacted by the IDRA in 18 U.S.C. § 17. The IDRA tightened the defense by requiring a "severe" mental disease, changing "substantial capacity" to a strict "unable to appreciate" standard, eliminating the volitional prong entirely, and shifting the burden of proof to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. It also correctly applies these rules to Markov, concluding that his severe schizophrenia and delusional inability to appreciate wrongfulness still satisfy this stricter threshold.
(e) Argument-for: A student might argue that the addition of the word "severe" in the IDRA was meant to drastically limit the defense to extreme, physically incapacitating conditions. Under this strict textualist view, unless a mental disease totally eliminates a defendant's physical volition or capacity to move, it does not meet the high federal bar of "severe." Thus, Markov's ability to physically coordinate an attack would automatically disqualify his schizophrenia.

Head-to-head: Option (d) is the only legally accurate statement of the IDRA rules, correctly listing its restrictive changes (burden shift, "severe" requirement, strict "unable" cognitive test, and elimination of the volitional prong) while accurately applying them to the facts. Options (a), (b), (c), and (e) rely on explicitly false claims about the text and application of the IDRA statute. Option (a) incorrectly claims the tests share the "substantial capacity" language. Option (b) falsely claims IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong (it retains "wrongfulness"). Option (c) falsely claims IDRA places the burden on the prosecution (it shifts it to the defense). Option (e) invents a non-existent legal requirement for "total physical incapacitation."

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests—both ask whether the defendant 'lacked substantial capacity'" — wrong because the IDRA explicitly uses the stricter "unable to appreciate" standard, rejecting the MPC's "substantial capacity" language.
- (b): "IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong" — wrong because 18 U.S.C. § 17(a) expressly retains the wrongfulness prong ("unable to appreciate the nature and quality or the wrongfulness of his acts").
- (c): "IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt" — wrong because 18 U.S.C. § 17(b) explicitly places the burden of proof on the defendant by clear and convincing evidence.
- (e): "excludes psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total physical incapacitation" — wrong because the IDRA's "severe" requirement does not mandate physical incapacitation; it was intended to exclude non-psychotic behavioral/personality disorders.

Recommended fix: none

## Q08

### Grounding

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense options because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings; therefore, psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose, even if the defendant lacked the ability to form intent.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense to ensure mentally ill defendants are not unjustly punished for their acts.
(c) Markov's mental illness could only be used to negate the mens rea element of assault; however, because his delusion about stopping an assassin confirms he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury, his psychiatric evidence regarding wrongfulness would be irrelevant.
(d) Markov would be acquitted because the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief that the underlying physical conduct was legally justified to protect others from an imminent harm.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability at the trial stage.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (*Kahler v. Kansas*), the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea. Markov understood he was striking a person to stop an assassination, meaning he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury. Therefore, his mental illness evidence only bears on his inability to appreciate wrongfulness, which is irrelevant and inadmissible under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental disease evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler* held the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness-based insanity defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on a delusional belief. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence bearing solely on moral wrongfulness.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Mens Rea Model Abolition / Kahler v. Kansas, Ch 23, Refinements `mens-rea-model-abolition` and `kahler-constitutional-floor`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Edge Case Audit

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense options because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings; therefore, psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose, even if the defendant lacked the ability to form intent.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense to ensure mentally ill defendants are not unjustly punished for their acts.
(c) Markov's mental illness could only be used to negate the mens rea element of assault; however, because his delusion about stopping an assassin confirms he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury, his psychiatric evidence regarding wrongfulness would be irrelevant.
(d) Markov would be acquitted because the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief that the underlying physical conduct was legally justified to protect others from an imminent harm.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability at the trial stage.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (*Kahler v. Kansas*), the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea. Markov understood he was striking a person to stop an assassination, meaning he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury. Therefore, his mental illness evidence only bears on his inability to appreciate wrongfulness, which is irrelevant and inadmissible under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental disease evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler* held the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness-based insanity defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on a delusional belief. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence bearing solely on moral wrongfulness.

<!-- edge-case-audit: CLEAN
1. Fact Pattern Booby Traps: pass
2. Cross-Doctrine Clashes: pass (Fact 6 expressly locks down objective reasonableness for self-defense/defense of others, preventing the delusion from forming a valid justification defense under a different theory).
3. Cross-Question Spoilers: pass
Recommended fix: n/a

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense options because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings; therefore, psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose, even if the defendant lacked the ability to form intent.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense to ensure mentally ill defendants are not unjustly punished for their acts.
(c) Markov's mental illness could only be used to negate the mens rea element of assault; however, because his delusion about stopping an assassin confirms he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury, his psychiatric evidence regarding wrongfulness would be irrelevant.
(d) Markov would be acquitted because the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief that the underlying physical conduct was legally justified to protect others from an imminent harm.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability at the trial stage.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (*Kahler v. Kansas*), the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea. Markov understood he was striking a person to stop an assassination, meaning he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury. Therefore, his mental illness evidence only bears on his inability to appreciate wrongfulness, which is irrelevant and inadmissible under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental disease evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler* held the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness-based insanity defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on a delusional belief. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence bearing solely on moral wrongfulness.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might reason that if a state has "abolished the insanity defense," it reflects a legislative judgment that mental illness has no place in assessing guilt. Thus, courts in those jurisdictions would logically exclude all psychiatric evidence to prevent the defense from sneaking insanity concepts through the back door.
(b) Argument-for: A student might incorrectly recall the outcome of *Kahler v. Kansas*, confusing the petitioner's argument with the Court's holding. Since the Eighth Amendment prohibits cruel and unusual punishment, one could persuasively but incorrectly argue the Supreme Court established a constitutional baseline requiring states to assess a defendant's capacity to recognize wrongfulness.
(c) Argument-for: This is the correct application of the mens rea model validated in *Kahler v. Kansas*. Since Markov intended the physical act of causing bodily injury to another person, he fulfilled the statutory mens rea of "purposely or knowingly." Because his delusion primarily impacts his understanding of whether the act was morally or legally wrong—an inquiry precluded by the abolition of the insanity defense—his evidence is inadmissible to defeat the charge.
(d) Argument-for: A student might conflate the mens rea model with a subjective justification defense. If Markov sincerely believed he was stopping an assassin, a student could argue he lacked the true "criminal intent" necessary for assault, reasoning that the mens rea model inherently accommodates delusional self-defense or defense of others.
(e) Argument-for: A student might assume that "abolishing the insanity defense" simply means downgrading it from an affirmative defense to an evidentiary rule where all psychiatric evidence is weighed by the jury to assess general moral culpability. Under this view, abolition merely changes the procedural mechanism, not the substantive relevance of wrongfulness.

Head-to-head: 
Option (c) accurately synthesizes the rule from *Kahler v. Kansas*: in a mens rea model jurisdiction, psychiatric evidence is only admissible if it defeats the specific mental state required by the statute, which a delusion of justification does not do. The distractors are decisively flawed. Option (a) falsely claims an absolute evidentiary bar. Option (b) legally reverses the actual holding of *Kahler*. Option (d) fabricates a complete affirmative defense under the mens rea model. Option (e) contradicts the core definition of an abolition jurisdiction by claiming moral wrongfulness evidence remains admissible. 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose" — wrong because mens rea model states explicitly allow this evidence to negate the statutory mens rea.
- (b): "the Supreme Court held in Kahler v. Kansas that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense" — wrong because *Kahler* held the exact opposite, ruling that Due Process does not mandate a wrongfulness test.
- (d): "the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief" — wrong because the mens rea model provides no such affirmative defense and solely limits the inquiry to the elements of the offense.
- (e): "abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability" — wrong because abolition states expressly prohibit evidence admitted solely to show an inability to appreciate moral wrongfulness.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense options because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings; therefore, psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose, even if the defendant lacked the ability to form intent.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense to ensure mentally ill defendants are not unjustly punished for their acts.
(c) Markov's mental illness could only be used to negate the mens rea element of assault; however, because his delusion about stopping an assassin confirms he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury, his psychiatric evidence regarding wrongfulness would be irrelevant.
(d) Markov would be acquitted because the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief that the underlying physical conduct was legally justified to protect others from an imminent harm.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability at the trial stage.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (*Kahler v. Kansas*), the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea. Markov understood he was striking a person to stop an assassination, meaning he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury. Therefore, his mental illness evidence only bears on his inability to appreciate wrongfulness, which is irrelevant and inadmissible under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental disease evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler* held the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness-based insanity defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on a delusional belief. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence bearing solely on moral wrongfulness.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

## Q09

### Grounding

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness. Under this view, the mere presence of a personality disorder acts as an absolute bar to any insanity claim.
(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient. This supposed merger doctrine prevents the prosecution from attempting to parse the relative causal contributions of the distinct psychiatric conditions.
(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which specific disorder actually "drove" the violent behavior completely legally irrelevant.
(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement. Therefore, if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense legally survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior. <!-- correct -->
(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role. Under this standard, any causal contribution from the personality disorder precludes a valid insanity plea.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC § 4.01(2), an abnormality manifested "only by repeated criminal or otherwise anti-social conduct" does not qualify as a mental disease. The word "only" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but it does not disqualify a defendant with a co-occurring mental illness. The exclusion applies to the threshold disease requirement, not causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder contributed. (a) is incorrect because the MPC excludes abnormalities manifested *only* by antisocial conduct; it is not an absolute bar for co-occurring conditions. (b) is incorrect because the MPC contains no "automatic merger" doctrine; the disorders remain legally distinct for the causation inquiry. (c) is incorrect because a co-occurring psychotic disorder does not automatically satisfy the causation element; the defense must still prove the qualifying disease caused the incapacity. (e) is incorrect because the statute does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause, nor does any contribution from a personality disorder defeat the defense entirely if the qualifying disease caused the requisite incapacity.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, personality-disorder-exclusion, co-occurring-causation, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students treat the personality disorder exclusion as a taint rule

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, personality-disorder-exclusion refinement; co-occurring-causation refinement; *State v. Meiser*.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — personality disorder exclusion / co-occurring causation, Ch 23, Refinement `personality-disorder-exclusion` and `co-occurring-causation`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that the MPC’s specific exclusion of psychopathic or antisocial personality traits in Section 4.01(2) acts as a strict character taint rule. Because the statute explicitly removes these abnormalities from the definition of a mental disease, any conduct driven by them cannot be excused. Therefore, the mere presence of an antisocial personality disorder acts as an absolute bar to the defense, forcing the defense to concede the point regardless of other diagnoses.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that psychiatry is imprecise and the law refuses to artificially bifurcate a single human mind. Under this reasoning, the defense should invoke a merger doctrine, arguing that the MPC does not allow parsing a defendant's intertwined diagnoses. Consequently, the co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category, preventing the prosecution from isolating the personality disorder as the sole driver of the behavior.
(c) Argument-for: A student might assert that severe mental illnesses like schizophrenia inherently override personality disorders in legal significance. Because a psychotic disorder fundamentally fractures reality, its presence renders any non-qualifying personality disorder legally moot. Under this view, the co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's attempt to isolate the personality disorder's causal role completely irrelevant.
(d) Argument-for: This is the correct application of the Model Penal Code. Under MPC § 4.01(2), the exclusion only applies to an abnormality manifested "only" by repeated antisocial conduct. Antisocial personality disorder alone does not qualify, but it does not disqualify a defendant who also suffers from schizophrenia. The exclusion governs the threshold definition of a mental disease, meaning if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the requisite incapacity, the defense remains valid even if there are concurrent causes.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that criminal law causation rules strictly require the legally recognized condition to be the exclusive cause of the harm. If the non-qualifying antisocial personality disorder was a but-for cause of the violent behavior, the qualifying mental disease fails the strict causation test. Under this standard, any causal contribution from the personality disorder breaks the chain, forcing the defense to concede an insurmountable causation problem.

Head-to-head: Option (d) relies on the exact statutory text of MPC § 4.01(2), properly distinguishing between the threshold definition of a mental disease and the subsequent causation inquiry. Options (a), (b), (c), and (e) all invent or distort legal doctrines, clearly flagged by their absolute language. Option (a) invents an absolute bar for personality disorders, directly contradicting the word "only" in the MPC. Option (b) invents an MPC "automatic merger" doctrine. Option (c) falsely claims that a psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes," negating the actual insanity test's causation requirement. Option (e) incorrectly imports a strict sole-cause requirement, falsely claiming that any "but-for" contribution from a non-qualifying disorder entirely defeats the defense.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness." — wrong because MPC § 4.01(2) only excludes abnormalities manifested "only" by antisocial conduct; it does not categorically exclude defendants with co-occurring qualifying diseases.
- (b): "the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category" — wrong because there is no such "automatic merger" provision in the Model Penal Code; the disorders remain analytically distinct.
- (c): "any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" — wrong because the defendant must still factually prove that the qualifying mental disease caused the specific lack of substantial capacity during the offense.
- (e): "the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely" — wrong because the MPC does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause of the conduct; concurrent causation by a personality disorder does not automatically preclude the defense.

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness. Under this view, the mere presence of a personality disorder acts as an absolute bar to any insanity claim.
(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient. This supposed merger doctrine prevents the prosecution from attempting to parse the relative causal contributions of the distinct psychiatric conditions.
(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which specific disorder actually "drove" the violent behavior completely legally irrelevant.
(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement. Therefore, if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense legally survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior. <!-- correct -->
(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role. Under this standard, any causal contribution from the personality disorder precludes a valid insanity plea.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC § 4.01(2), an abnormality manifested "only by repeated criminal or otherwise anti-social conduct" does not qualify as a mental disease. The word "only" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but it does not disqualify a defendant with a co-occurring mental illness. The exclusion applies to the threshold disease requirement, not causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder contributed. (a) is incorrect because the MPC excludes abnormalities manifested *only* by antisocial conduct; it is not an absolute bar for co-occurring conditions. (b) is incorrect because the MPC contains no "automatic merger" doctrine; the disorders remain legally distinct for the causation inquiry. (c) is incorrect because a co-occurring psychotic disorder does not automatically satisfy the causation element; the defense must still prove the qualifying disease caused the incapacity. (e) is incorrect because the statute does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause, nor does any contribution from a personality disorder defeat the defense entirely if the qualifying disease caused the requisite incapacity.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, personality-disorder-exclusion, co-occurring-causation, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students treat the personality disorder exclusion as a taint rule

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, personality-disorder-exclusion refinement; co-occurring-causation refinement; *State v. Meiser*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that the MPC’s specific exclusion of psychopathic or antisocial personality traits in Section 4.01(2) acts as a strict character taint rule. Because the statute explicitly removes these abnormalities from the definition of a mental disease, any conduct driven by them cannot be excused. Therefore, the mere presence of an antisocial personality disorder acts as an absolute bar to the defense, forcing the defense to concede the point regardless of other diagnoses.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that psychiatry is imprecise and the law refuses to artificially bifurcate a single human mind. Under this reasoning, the defense should invoke a merger doctrine, arguing that the MPC does not allow parsing a defendant's intertwined diagnoses. Consequently, the co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category, preventing the prosecution from isolating the personality disorder as the sole driver of the behavior.
(c) Argument-for: A student might assert that severe mental illnesses like schizophrenia inherently override personality disorders in legal significance. Because a psychotic disorder fundamentally fractures reality, its presence renders any non-qualifying personality disorder legally moot. Under this view, the co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's attempt to isolate the personality disorder's causal role completely irrelevant.
(d) Argument-for: This is the correct application of the Model Penal Code. Under MPC § 4.01(2), the exclusion only applies to an abnormality manifested "only" by repeated antisocial conduct. Antisocial personality disorder alone does not qualify, but it does not disqualify a defendant who also suffers from schizophrenia. The exclusion governs the threshold definition of a mental disease, meaning if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the requisite incapacity, the defense remains valid even if there are concurrent causes.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that criminal law causation rules strictly require the legally recognized condition to be the exclusive cause of the harm. If the non-qualifying antisocial personality disorder was a but-for cause of the violent behavior, the qualifying mental disease fails the strict causation test. Under this standard, any causal contribution from the personality disorder breaks the chain, forcing the defense to concede an insurmountable causation problem.

Head-to-head: Option (d) relies on the exact statutory text of MPC § 4.01(2), properly distinguishing between the threshold definition of a mental disease and the subsequent causation inquiry. Options (a), (b), (c), and (e) all invent or distort legal doctrines, clearly flagged by their absolute language. Option (a) invents an absolute bar for personality disorders, directly contradicting the word "only" in the MPC. Option (b) invents an MPC "automatic merger" doctrine. Option (c) falsely claims that a psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes," negating the actual insanity test's causation requirement. Option (e) incorrectly imports a strict sole-cause requirement, falsely claiming that any "but-for" contribution from a non-qualifying disorder entirely defeats the defense.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness." — wrong because MPC § 4.01(2) only excludes abnormalities manifested "only" by antisocial conduct; it does not categorically exclude defendants with co-occurring qualifying diseases.
- (b): "the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category" — wrong because there is no such "automatic merger" provision in the Model Penal Code; the disorders remain analytically distinct.
- (c): "any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" — wrong because the defendant must still factually prove that the qualifying mental disease caused the specific lack of substantial capacity during the offense.
- (e): "the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely" — wrong because the MPC does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause of the conduct; concurrent causation by a personality disorder does not automatically preclude the defense.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q10

### Grounding

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to a murder charge for the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. This categorical prohibition operates as an absolute substantive bar to the defense at the guilt phase of the trial.

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions. Under this view, the credibility of the threat to his mother outweighs the common law homicide bar, allowing the jury to fully acquit him at trial.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised. Even if the threat was highly credible, the defense fails categorically because it lacks this strictly required element.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense. This exception serves to protect coerced actors from severe homicide consequences.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder. This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration; it is actually a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase. Option (c) relies on the MPC rule despite Fact 6 specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend extending the defense to homicide. Option (d) contains a false legal rule: the common law does not require the threat to target the defendant personally and extends duress to threats against close relations. Option (e) falsely claims that duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability; this exception exists only in a small minority of states and is not the universal common law rule.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duress murder bar, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-murder-bar`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to a murder charge for the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. This categorical prohibition operates as an absolute substantive bar to the defense at the guilt phase of the trial.

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions. Under this view, the credibility of the threat to his mother outweighs the common law homicide bar, allowing the jury to fully acquit him at trial.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised. Even if the threat was highly credible, the defense fails categorically because it lacks this strictly required element.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense. This exception serves to protect coerced actors from severe homicide consequences.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder. This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration; it is actually a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase. Option (c) relies on the MPC rule despite Fact 6 specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend extending the defense to homicide. Option (d) contains a false legal rule: the common law does not require the threat to target the defendant personally and extends duress to threats against close relations. Option (e) falsely claims that duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability; this exception exists only in a small minority of states and is not the universal common law rule.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that the strict common law rule forbidding duress as a murder defense has been conceptually reimagined in some contexts as an imperfect defense or a sentencing mitigator. To avoid a profoundly unfair result at the guilt phase, courts sometimes treat mitigating factors as reducing culpability rather than barring liability completely. By stating the "murder bar" operates only to limit exposure after conviction, this option offers a plausible compromise. It reconciles the rigid common law tradition with the reality of a highly credible threat to a close relative.
(b) Argument-for: This accurately reflects the core traditional common law doctrine. Under the common law, duress is simply not a defense to intentional murder, nor is it a defense to felony murder. The fundamental rationale is that the law cannot endorse balancing an innocent life against another. Therefore, the defense is categorically unavailable as a substantive bar at the guilt phase. This applies regardless of how severe, imminent, or credible the threat is.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that the Model Penal Code represents the definitive modern understanding of duress. Under this view, the MPC's progressive approach has effectively eclipsed the traditional common law in contemporary practice. By pointing to a "modern trend" that allows duress for all crimes, this option appeals to students who believe the common law's rigid murder bar has been universally abandoned. If it were true that all jurisdictions allow this, the jury could fully acquit Holmes.
(d) Argument-for: A student might recall that strict historical common law rules required the threat of harm to be strictly imminent and direct. They could reasonably deduce that the threat must be to the defendant himself rather than a third party to satisfy this immediacy. By arguing that the common law "requires that the threat target the defendant personally," this option provides a technically strict rule-based reason to deny the defense. This sounds like an authentic common law technicality that categorically defeats the defense.
(e) Argument-for: A student could reason that felony murder strictly requires an underlying predicate felony to anchor liability. If that underlying felony is completely excused by duress, the felony murder charge must logically fail because its foundation is gone. By stating that duress "always breaks the chain of felony murder liability," this option provides a compelling logical syllogism. The claim that this happens in "every jurisdiction" sounds like a definitive hornbook rule that cleanly resolves the tension between the murder bar and a coerced predicate act.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the clear, unambiguous correct answer, correctly applying the traditional common law rule that completely bars duress as a defense to any murder. The distractors are remarkably well-constructed because they all anchor into plausible misunderstandings of doctrine, yet they all feature explicitly false, locked legal statements. Option (a) incorrectly claims the murder bar is just a sentencing-phase consideration. Option (c) applies the MPC despite the prompt specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend in "all jurisdictions." Option (d) falsely claims the common law strictly requires the threat to target the defendant personally. Option (e) uses absolute phrasing to falsely claim duress "always" breaks the felony murder chain in "every jurisdiction," which fundamentally conflicts with the traditional common law rule.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the common law's 'murder bar' is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction... not a substantive bar at the guilt phase" — wrong because the common law murder bar is an absolute substantive defense bar at the guilt phase.
- (c): "the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions" — wrong because many jurisdictions, specifically those following the common law, continue to maintain a strict bar against duress for murder.
- (d): "the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally" — wrong because the common law broadly allows duress to be claimed for threats of death or serious bodily injury to immediate family members or close relations.
- (e): "duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction" — wrong because the traditional common law rule (and the majority rule) is that duress is categorically unavailable for murder, including felony murder, meaning it does not break the chain in every jurisdiction.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to a murder charge for the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. This categorical prohibition operates as an absolute substantive bar to the defense at the guilt phase of the trial.

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions. Under this view, the credibility of the threat to his mother outweighs the common law homicide bar, allowing the jury to fully acquit him at trial.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised. Even if the threat was highly credible, the defense fails categorically because it lacks this strictly required element.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense. This exception serves to protect coerced actors from severe homicide consequences.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder. This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration; it is actually a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase. Option (c) relies on the MPC rule despite Fact 6 specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend extending the defense to homicide. Option (d) contains a false legal rule: the common law does not require the threat to target the defendant personally and extends duress to threats against close relations. Option (e) falsely claims that duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability; this exception exists only in a small minority of states and is not the universal common law rule.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.
