## Q11

### Grounding

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim, foreclosing judicial weighing of the harms.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break clearly outweighs the minor property damage caused by the break-in, and the immediate unavailability of an ER bed fully satisfies the strict no-legal-alternative element for both.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency.
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis, regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury, whereas a psychotic break is merely a severe medical condition.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a common-law rule; necessity compares the magnitude of harms and does not categorically require the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Necessity fault calibration (MPC vs. Common Law), Ch 21, Refinement `mpc-fault-calibration`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Edge Case Audit

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim, foreclosing judicial weighing of the harms.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break clearly outweighs the minor property damage caused by the break-in, and the immediate unavailability of an ER bed fully satisfies the strict no-legal-alternative element for both.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency.
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis, regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury, whereas a psychotic break is merely a severe medical condition.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a common-law rule; necessity compares the magnitude of harms and does not categorically require the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

1. Fact Pattern Booby Traps: pass
2. Cross-Doctrine Clashes: pass
3. Cross-Question Spoilers: pass
Recommended fix: n/a
-->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim, foreclosing judicial weighing of the harms.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break clearly outweighs the minor property damage caused by the break-in, and the immediate unavailability of an ER bed fully satisfies the strict no-legal-alternative element for both.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency.
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis, regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury, whereas a psychotic break is merely a severe medical condition.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a common-law rule; necessity compares the magnitude of harms and does not categorically require the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative*, the Supreme Court held that a medical necessity defense cannot succeed when the legislature has already weighed the harms by classifying a substance under the Controlled Substances Act. Since Kant is charged with unauthorized possession of a controlled substance, a student could argue that the legislative scheduling categorically preempts the judicial weighing of harms required for a necessity defense. In this view, any claim of medical necessity is foreclosed by the statutory scheme.
(b) Argument-for: The core of the necessity defense is the choice of evils. A student could argue that averting a severe medical crisis like a psychotic break clearly outweighs the minor property damage of breaking into a pharmacy and the victimless crime of possessing a drug. Since Kant had no legal alternative (assuming no ER beds were available), the defense's primary elements are satisfied. Thus, the defense should be viable under both the common law and MPC, treating his failure to renew his prescription as mere background context rather than an absolute bar.
(c) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the MPC's nuanced approach to fault in creating the emergency. Under traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the crisis entirely bars the necessity defense. However, MPC § 3.02(2) calibrates fault: if the defendant is negligent or reckless in bringing about the situation, the defense is only barred for crimes where negligence or recklessness suffices. Because Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing possession, his lesser fault in creating the emergency does not bar his defense.
(d) Argument-for: A student could interpret the MPC's fault provision as a strict, categorical rule against rewarding defendants who create their own emergencies. If a defendant's negligence or recklessness brings about the crisis, they shouldn't be allowed to invoke necessity to escape punishment for subsequent intentional acts. Therefore, one could argue that § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is at fault, regardless of the mens rea of the ultimate charge.
(e) Argument-for: Common law justification defenses are often narrower than the MPC. A student could conflate the elements of necessity with duress, or assume that traditional courts strictly limited necessity to physical emergencies. Under this reasoning, common law necessity strictly requires an imminent threat of death or serious bodily injury, meaning a psychiatric emergency like a psychotic break would only qualify under the broader "choice of evils" standard of the MPC.

Head-to-head: Option (c) accurately captures the legal distinction between the common law's absolute fault bar and the MPC's mens rea-calibrated fault rule. Option (a) overextends *Oakland Cannabis*, which only applies to Schedule I drugs (where Congress explicitly found "no currently accepted medical use"), not standard prescription drugs. Option (b) falsely claims viability under both standards, ignoring the common-law absolute bar for creating the emergency. Option (d) misstates the MPC rule by making it a categorical bar, directly contradicting the statutory text of § 3.02(2). Option (e) falsely imports the "death or serious bodily injury" requirement from duress into common-law necessity, which generally allows any lesser harm to be chosen over a greater harm. All distractors contain clear, falsifiable legal errors locked with absolute phrasing.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim" — wrong because *Oakland Cannabis* applies only to Schedule I drugs (which have "no currently accepted medical use" by definition), not to the mere scheduling of any prescription drug.
- (b): "viable under both standards" — wrong because the traditional common law categorically bars the necessity defense if the defendant contributed to creating the emergency.
- (d): "categorically strips the justification defense... regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime" — wrong because MPC § 3.02(2) explicitly preserves the necessity defense for intentional/knowing crimes if the defendant was merely reckless/negligent in creating the crisis.
- (e): "traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury" — wrong because common-law necessity compares the relative magnitude of harms and is not strictly limited to threats of death or serious bodily injury.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim, foreclosing judicial weighing of the harms.

(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency.
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis, regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury, whereas a psychotic break is merely a severe medical condition.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. ---

## Q12

### Grounding

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code.
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which strictly limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally, meaning any coercive pressure exerted by threatening a relative is deemed legally insufficient.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not strictly imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation days later rather than immediate on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement to ensure the threshold element rests on an unavoidable present danger.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code's broader standard but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, meaning that mere verbal threats of a severe beating by an enforcer cannot ever qualify as serious bodily injury for this defense.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats clearly do not satisfy the required "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person with five days' notice would have reported these threats to the police before March 15, thereby immediately rendering the underlying threat insufficient as a strict matter of law prior to trial.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duress, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-threat-threshold` and `duress-target-scope`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." ### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code.
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which strictly limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally, meaning any coercive pressure exerted by threatening a relative is deemed legally insufficient.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not strictly imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation days later rather than immediate on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement to ensure the threshold element rests on an unavoidable present danger.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code's broader standard but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, meaning that mere verbal threats of a severe beating by an enforcer cannot ever qualify as serious bodily injury for this defense.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats clearly do not satisfy the required "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person with five days' notice would have reported these threats to the police before March 15, thereby immediately rendering the underlying threat insufficient as a strict matter of law prior to trial.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Option (a) accurately applies the black-letter rules for duress under both the common law and the Model Penal Code. Under the common law, the threat must be of death or serious bodily injury, and a week-long hospital stay inflicted by a known violent enforcer clearly qualifies as serious bodily harm. Furthermore, the common law traditionally recognizes threats against a "close relation," such as a parent. Finally, the MPC's broader standard requires only a threat of "unlawful force" against "any person," which is easily satisfied by Bentham's threats against both Kant and his mother.

(b) Argument-for: Option (b) could be argued as correct by appealing to the strictest historical common-law formulations of duress, which demanded that the threat of death or great bodily harm be directed at the defendant himself. A student could argue that extending the scope to third parties is a modern MPC innovation rather than a universal common-law rule. By this logic, the coercive pressure from a threat against Kant's mother fails the target scope element. Therefore, any reliance on the threat to the mother is legally insufficient under an orthodox strict reading of the common law.

(c) Argument-for: Option (c) draws upon the fact that the gap between March 10 and March 15 strongly implicates imminence and the opportunity to escape. A student could argue that while the MPC alters the formulation of the defense, it effectively retains the imminence requirement through the "person of reasonable firmness" standard, as no reasonable person would yield to a non-imminent threat without seeking help. Because Bentham promised retaliation days later rather than immediate on-the-spot force, the threat lacked immediacy. Thus, the threshold fails under the MPC because the danger was not an unavoidable present danger.

(d) Argument-for: Option (d) asserts a rigid interpretation of the common-law threshold for duress, which was historically reserved for extreme, immediate threats of death. A student might argue that a mere verbal threat of a beating—even a severe one by an enforcer—does not rise to the level of death or the kind of catastrophic bodily harm traditionally required, such as a literal gun to the head. Because the common law strongly disfavors the duress defense, this strict threshold ensures that only the most undeniably overpowering threats qualify. Therefore, Bentham's threat falls short of the common-law standard but satisfies the MPC's "unlawful force" standard.

(e) Argument-for: Option (e) focuses on the five-day gap between the threat and the crime, arguing that this delay defeats the defense under both standards as a strict matter of law. Under the common law, a five-day gap provides ample reasonable opportunity to escape or report to the police, which operates as an absolute bar. Under the MPC, a person of "reasonable firmness" would undoubtedly report a five-day advance threat to authorities rather than commit a bank robbery. Because the facts establish a five-day notice period, a judge would preclude the defense before it ever reaches the jury, making both thresholds fail strictly as a matter of law.

Head-to-head: Option (a) accurately aligns the facts with the standard elements for duress under both the common law and the MPC. Option (b) invents a falsifiable claim that the common law "strictly limits" cognizable threats to the defendant personally, explicitly contradicting the accepted common-law inclusion of "close relations." Option (c) fails because it asserts the MPC "ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement," which is a direct misstatement of MPC § 2.09. Option (d) uses absolutist phrasing ("strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario") to falsely claim that severe beatings cannot satisfy the common-law serious bodily injury threshold. Option (e) falsely collapses the "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element into the threat threshold and incorrectly asserts that a five-day delay automatically precludes the MPC defense "as a strict matter of law prior to trial," stripping the jury of its fact-finding role on "reasonable firmness." All distractors are firmly locked with explicit false legal claims.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "strictly limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally" — wrong because the common law expressly recognizes threats directed at "close relations" such as parents, spouses, and children.
- (c): "the Model Penal Code ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement" — wrong because the MPC deliberately abandons the strict imminence requirement in favor of a general "person of reasonable firmness" standard.
- (d): "strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, meaning that mere verbal threats of a severe beating... cannot ever qualify" — wrong because the common law explicitly accepts threats of "serious bodily injury" or "grievous bodily harm," not just threats of death or gun-to-the-head scenarios.
- (e): "immediately rendering the underlying threat insufficient as a strict matter of law prior to trial" — wrong because time delay relates to the distinct "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element at common law (not the threat threshold), and presents a factual jury question regarding "reasonable firmness" under the MPC rather than an automatic legal bar prior to trial.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code.
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which strictly limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally, meaning any coercive pressure exerted by threatening a relative is deemed legally insufficient.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not strictly imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation days later rather than immediate on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement to ensure the threshold element rests on an unavoidable present danger.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code's broader standard but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, meaning that mere verbal threats of a severe beating by an enforcer cannot ever qualify as serious bodily injury for this defense.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats clearly do not satisfy the required "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person with five days' notice would have reported these threats to the police before March 15, thereby immediately rendering the underlying threat insufficient as a strict matter of law prior to trial.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Option (a) accurately applies the black-letter rules for duress under both the common law and the Model Penal Code. Under the common law, the threat must be of death or serious bodily injury, and a week-long hospital stay inflicted by a known violent enforcer clearly qualifies as serious bodily harm. Furthermore, the common law traditionally recognizes threats against a "close relation," such as a parent. Finally, the MPC's broader standard requires only a threat of "unlawful force" against "any person," which is easily satisfied by Bentham's threats against both Kant and his mother.

(b) Argument-for: Option (b) could be argued as correct by appealing to the strictest historical common-law formulations of duress, which demanded that the threat of death or great bodily harm be directed at the defendant himself. A student could argue that extending the scope to third parties is a modern MPC innovation rather than a universal common-law rule. By this logic, the coercive pressure from a threat against Kant's mother fails the target scope element. Therefore, any reliance on the threat to the mother is legally insufficient under an orthodox strict reading of the common law.

(c) Argument-for: Option (c) draws upon the fact that the gap between March 10 and March 15 strongly implicates imminence and the opportunity to escape. A student could argue that while the MPC alters the formulation of the defense, it effectively retains the imminence requirement through the "person of reasonable firmness" standard, as no reasonable person would yield to a non-imminent threat without seeking help. Because Bentham promised retaliation days later rather than immediate on-the-spot force, the threat lacked immediacy. Thus, the threshold fails under the MPC because the danger was not an unavoidable present danger.

(d) Argument-for: Option (d) asserts a rigid interpretation of the common-law threshold for duress, which was historically reserved for extreme, immediate threats of death. A student might argue that a mere verbal threat of a beating—even a severe one by an enforcer—does not rise to the level of death or the kind of catastrophic bodily harm traditionally required, such as a literal gun to the head. Because the common law strongly disfavors the duress defense, this strict threshold ensures that only the most undeniably overpowering threats qualify. Therefore, Bentham's threat falls short of the common-law standard but satisfies the MPC's "unlawful force" standard.

(e) Argument-for: Option (e) focuses on the five-day gap between the threat and the crime, arguing that this delay defeats the defense under both standards as a strict matter of law. Under the common law, a five-day gap provides ample reasonable opportunity to escape or report to the police, which operates as an absolute bar. Under the MPC, a person of "reasonable firmness" would undoubtedly report a five-day advance threat to authorities rather than commit a bank robbery. Because the facts establish a five-day notice period, a judge would preclude the defense before it ever reaches the jury, making both thresholds fail strictly as a matter of law.

Head-to-head: Option (a) accurately aligns the facts with the standard elements for duress under both the common law and the MPC. Option (b) invents a falsifiable claim that the common law "strictly limits" cognizable threats to the defendant personally, explicitly contradicting the accepted common-law inclusion of "close relations." Option (c) fails because it asserts the MPC "ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement," which is a direct misstatement of MPC § 2.09. Option (d) uses absolutist phrasing ("strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario") to falsely claim that severe beatings cannot satisfy the common-law serious bodily injury threshold. Option (e) falsely collapses the "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element into the threat threshold and incorrectly asserts that a five-day delay automatically precludes the MPC defense "as a strict matter of law prior to trial," stripping the jury of its fact-finding role on "reasonable firmness." All distractors are firmly locked with explicit false legal claims.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "strictly limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally" — wrong because the common law expressly recognizes threats directed at "close relations" such as parents, spouses, and children.
- (c): "the Model Penal Code ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement" — wrong because the MPC deliberately abandons the strict imminence requirement in favor of a general "person of reasonable firmness" standard.
- (d): "strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, meaning that mere verbal threats of a severe beating... cannot ever qualify" — wrong because the common law explicitly accepts threats of "serious bodily injury" or "grievous bodily harm," not just threats of death or gun-to-the-head scenarios.
- (e): "immediately rendering the underlying threat insufficient as a strict matter of law prior to trial" — wrong because time delay relates to the distinct "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element at common law (not the threat threshold), and presents a factual jury question regarding "reasonable firmness" under the MPC rather than an automatic legal bar prior to trial.

Recommended fix: none

## Q13

### Grounding

**Q13.** Assume Kant is charged with murder for Bayes's death based on his participation in the armored-car robbery. Relying solely on the provided text of Model Penal Code § 2.09, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Kant's potential duress defense to the murder charge?

(a) Kant's duress defense is categorically barred because the Model Penal Code implicitly contains a strict prohibition against asserting this affirmative excuse for any homicide offense, requiring Kant to face felony murder liability regardless of the threat's severity or his resistance.
(b) Kant's duress defense is fully available to be presented to the jury because Section 2.09 contains no categorical homicide exception, allowing the defense for any crime if a person of reasonable firmness in Kant's exact situation would have been unable to resist the physical threat. <!-- correct -->
(c) Kant's duress defense is strictly barred because Section 2.09 expressly limits the defense to situations where the unlawful force is threatened against the actor personally, meaning the statute categorically precludes any excuse based on severe threats directed toward family members.
(d) Kant's duress defense is fully available but only if he can prove the harm avoided (injury to his mother) quantitatively outweighed the harm caused (death of the guard), because the Model Penal Code merges duress into the choice of evils justification test for all homicide charges.
(e) Kant's duress defense is automatically successful because the Model Penal Code explicitly provides that any threat of serious bodily injury by an armed co-felon creates an irrebuttable presumption that a reasonable person would comply, shielding non-shooter participants from murder.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Model Penal Code § 2.09 contains no categorical exception for murder, allowing the defense for any "offense," including homicide, if a person of reasonable firmness in the actor's situation would have been unable to resist the threat. Option (a) is incorrect because the MPC text provided does not contain or imply a homicide exception. Option (c) is incorrect because § 2.09 expressly extends the defense to threats against "the person of another," not just the defendant personally. Option (d) is incorrect because the MPC treats duress (§ 2.09) as a distinct excuse evaluated under a "reasonable firmness" standard, rather than merging it into the § 3.02 choice of evils justification test which requires a strict quantitative balancing of harms. Option (e) is incorrect because the MPC does not create an irrebuttable presumption for armed co-felon threats; the jury must always evaluate the coercion under the objective "reasonable firmness" standard.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, mpc-reasonable-firmness], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (Model Penal Code § 2.09 abandons the CL murder bar; "person of another" scope; reasonable firmness standard).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Duress (murder bar and reasonable firmness standard), Ch 21, Refinements `duress-murder-bar`, `reasonable-firmness-objective`, `duress-target-scope`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q13.** Assume Kant is charged with murder for Bayes's death based on his participation in the armored-car robbery. Relying solely on the provided text of Model Penal Code § 2.09, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Kant's potential duress defense to the murder charge?

(a) Kant's duress defense is categorically barred because the Model Penal Code implicitly contains a strict prohibition against asserting this affirmative excuse for any homicide offense, requiring Kant to face felony murder liability regardless of the threat's severity or his resistance.
(b) Kant's duress defense is fully available to be presented to the jury because Section 2.09 contains no categorical homicide exception, allowing the defense for any crime if a person of reasonable firmness in Kant's exact situation would have been unable to resist the physical threat. <!-- correct -->
(c) Kant's duress defense is strictly barred because Section 2.09 expressly limits the defense to situations where the unlawful force is threatened against the actor personally, meaning the statute categorically precludes any excuse based on severe threats directed toward family members.
(d) Kant's duress defense is fully available but only if he can prove the harm avoided (injury to his mother) quantitatively outweighed the harm caused (death of the guard), because the Model Penal Code merges duress into the choice of evils justification test for all homicide charges.
(e) Kant's duress defense is automatically successful because the Model Penal Code explicitly provides that any threat of serious bodily injury by an armed co-felon creates an irrebuttable presumption that a reasonable person would comply, shielding non-shooter participants from murder.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Model Penal Code § 2.09 contains no categorical exception for murder, allowing the defense for any "offense," including homicide, if a person of reasonable firmness in the actor's situation would have been unable to resist the threat. Option (a) is incorrect because the MPC text provided does not contain or imply a homicide exception. Option (c) is incorrect because § 2.09 expressly extends the defense to threats against "the person of another," not just the defendant personally. Option (d) is incorrect because the MPC treats duress (§ 2.09) as a distinct excuse evaluated under a "reasonable firmness" standard, rather than merging it into the § 3.02 choice of evils justification test which requires a strict quantitative balancing of harms. Option (e) is incorrect because the MPC does not create an irrebuttable presumption for armed co-felon threats; the jury must always evaluate the coercion under the objective "reasonable firmness" standard.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, mpc-reasonable-firmness], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (Model Penal Code § 2.09 abandons the CL murder bar; "person of another" scope; reasonable firmness standard).

<!-- argument-pass: SHOULD FIX
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that because the MPC does not explicitly list homicide as a covered offense under § 2.09, and because the common law categorically barred duress for murder, the MPC implicitly retains this bar. Furthermore, the phrasing "implicitly contains a strict prohibition" appeals to students who remember that many jurisdictions refuse to allow duress for murder and mistakenly attribute this common law rule to the MPC.
(b) Argument-for: The Model Penal Code deliberately departed from the common law by abolishing the murder exception for duress. Section 2.09 covers any "offense" without explicitly excepting homicide. Therefore, a student would correctly conclude that as long as the objective "reasonable firmness" standard is met, the duress defense is fully available to a murder charge.
(c) Argument-for: A student could select this option if they confuse the MPC's broad scope with narrower, older common law standards that required the threat to be against the defendant personally. The argument would assert that criminal excuses must be strictly construed, meaning threats to absent third parties like family members do not legally suffice to excuse murder under this specific statutory provision.
(d) Argument-for: A student might conflate duress (an excuse) with necessity/choice of evils (a justification). They could argue that because murder involves taking an innocent life, the MPC requires the defendant to prove that the harm avoided outweighed the harm caused, effectively merging duress into the § 3.02 choice of evils test to prevent absurd results.
(e) Argument-for: Students might believe that armed robbery presents such an inherently dangerous and coercive environment that the MPC provides special categorical rules for non-shooters. They could argue that an "irrebuttable presumption" applies to armed co-felon threats to protect minor participants from the harshness of the felony murder rule, confusing duress principles with statutory felony murder affirmative defenses.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is clearly the correct answer, as it accurately reflects MPC § 2.09's departure from the common law murder exception and correctly identifies the "reasonable firmness" standard. Options (a), (c), (d), and (e) are excellent distractors because they test common misconceptions about the MPC's differences from the common law and the distinction between justification and excuse. Each distractor is strictly locked with falsifiable, absolute language ("implicitly contains a strict prohibition," "expressly limits," "merges," "explicitly provides"). The distractors operate perfectly under the close-call standard. However, the question stem references "the provided text of Model Penal Code § 2.09," which is missing from the prompt text here.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "implicitly contains a strict prohibition against asserting this affirmative excuse for any homicide offense" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 applies broadly to any "offense" and expressly abolishes the common law murder bar.
- (c): "expressly limits the defense to situations where the unlawful force is threatened against the actor personally" — wrong because § 2.09 explicitly extends the defense to threats against "the person of another."
- (d): "merges duress into the choice of evils justification test for all homicide charges" — wrong because the MPC treats duress (§ 2.09) and choice of evils (§ 3.02) as distinct defenses; they do not merge, and duress does not require a strict quantitative balancing.
- (e): "explicitly provides that any threat of serious bodily injury by an armed co-felon creates an irrebuttable presumption" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 contains no such presumption, instead relying on the jury's assessment of a person of "reasonable firmness."

Recommended fix: Remove the phrase "the provided text of" from the question stem if the statute is not supplied in the prompt, or ensure the text of MPC § 2.09 is included in the exam appendix/fact pattern. The distractors themselves are flawless.

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q13.** Assume Kant is charged with murder for Bayes's death based on his participation in the armored-car robbery. Relying solely on the provided text of Model Penal Code § 2.09, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Kant's potential duress defense to the murder charge?

(a) Kant's duress defense is categorically barred because the Model Penal Code implicitly contains a strict prohibition against asserting this affirmative excuse for any homicide offense, requiring Kant to face felony murder liability regardless of the threat's severity or his resistance.
(b) Kant's duress defense is fully available to be presented to the jury because Section 2.09 contains no categorical homicide exception, allowing the defense for any crime if a person of reasonable firmness in Kant's exact situation would have been unable to resist the physical threat. <!-- correct -->

Option (e) is incorrect because the MPC does not create an irrebuttable presumption for armed co-felon threats; the jury must always evaluate the coercion under the objective "reasonable firmness" standard.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might choose this by confusing the Model Penal Code with the common law. Under the common law, duress is categorically barred as a defense to intentional (and often felony) murder. If a student assumes the MPC "implicitly" retained this bedrock common law rule because homicide is uniquely heinous, they would confidently select this option.
(b) Argument-for: This accurately reflects the Model Penal Code's approach in § 2.09. (c) Argument-for: At common law, some formulations of duress rigidly restricted the defense to threats directed at the defendant personally or immediate family. Under MPC § 3.02, choice of evils requires that the harm avoided strictly outweigh the harm caused. - (e): "explicitly provides that any threat of serious bodily injury by an armed co-felon creates an irrebuttable presumption" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 contains no such explicit statutory presumption, leaving the "reasonable firmness" evaluation to the factfinder.

## Q14

### Grounding

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at Kant's chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. Prior to the robbery, Bentham had explicitly threatened to have Kant put in the hospital for a week. Assume that, whether or not Kant was the initial aggressor, his killing of Bentham proceeded to a jury on self-defense. Under New York's reasonableness standard as articulated in *People v. Goetz*, evaluate whether Kant's belief that deadly force was immediately necessary was reasonable.

(a) Kant's belief was reasonable because *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation, which asks only whether the defendant himself regarded his response as warranted. Under this strictly subjective view, as long as Kant genuinely feared Bentham's leveled revolver and remembered the hospitalization threat, the jury cannot second-guess his defensive action using an external, objective standard.

(b) A reasonable person in Kant's situation — facing Bentham leveling a revolver at his chest from six feet, with the only exit fifteen feet away without cover, and with knowledge of Bentham's explicit threat of hospitalizing violence — could reasonably believe deadly force was immediately necessary; *Goetz* permits the factfinder to incorporate the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker in the objective inquiry.

(c) Kant's belief was unreasonable because the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker or the attacker's reputation for violence; those are subjective elements that would convert the reasonable-person test into a defendant-specific inquiry. Consequently, the jury may consider the distance of six feet and the revolver, but must ignore Bentham's explicit threat.

(d) Kant's belief was reasonable because New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation, which asks only whether the defendant believed deadly force was necessary, without requiring objective reasonableness. Under this specific framework, Kant's honest fear of the leveled revolver and prior threat satisfies the justification, precluding the jury from assessing his defensive reaction against a hypothetical objective baseline.

(e) Kant's belief was reasonable because the controlling inquiry is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist firing, and Bentham's leveled revolver and explicit threat plainly overcome that threshold. This test evaluates whether the severe situational pressure of facing a gun from six feet without cover would compel a person to break the law, thereby excusing Kant's lethal response against the aggressor.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly applies *Goetz*, which held that reasonableness incorporates the defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and specific knowledge of the attacker — not a purely external, context-stripped inquiry. The leveled revolver from six feet, the absence of any safe exit, and Bentham's prior explicit threat of hospitalizing violence all inform what a reasonable person in Kant's situation would have perceived. (a) falsely attributes the "reasonable to him" formulation to *Goetz*; the Court of Appeals expressly rejected that Appellate Division reading and retained an objective element. (c) falsely excludes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker; *Goetz* expressly included them. (d) falsely states that NY follows the Model Penal Code; *Goetz* noted that the legislature "crucially inserted the word 'reasonably' before 'believes,'" rejecting the Model Penal Code's purely subjective approach. (e) imports the Model Penal Code duress standard ("person of reasonable firmness" in § 2.09) into a self-defense question; self-defense asks about the reasonableness of the defensive belief, not about firmness in resisting coercion.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, Goetz], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part II (*People v. Goetz*, 68 N.Y.2d 96 (1986): objective standard incorporates defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and knowledge of attacker; rejection of purely subjective Model Penal Code-style test).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Goetz objective standard and situational incorporation, Ch 22, Refinement `situational-incorporation`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at Kant's chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. Prior to the robbery, Bentham had explicitly threatened to have Kant put in the hospital for a week. Assume that, whether or not Kant was the initial aggressor, his killing of Bentham proceeded to a jury on self-defense. Under New York's reasonableness standard as articulated in *People v. (a) Kant's belief was reasonable because *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation, which asks only whether the defendant himself regarded his response as warranted. (b) A reasonable person in Kant's situation — facing Bentham leveling a revolver at his chest from six feet, with the only exit fifteen feet away without cover, and with knowledge of Bentham's explicit threat of hospitalizing violence — could reasonably believe deadly force was immediately necessary; *Goetz* permits the factfinder to incorporate the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker in the objective inquiry.

(c) Kant's belief was unreasonable because the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker or the attacker's reputation for violence; those are subjective elements that would convert the reasonable-person test into a defendant-specific inquiry. (d) Kant's belief was reasonable because New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation, which asks only whether the defendant believed deadly force was necessary, without requiring objective reasonableness. Under this specific framework, Kant's honest fear of the leveled revolver and prior threat satisfies the justification, precluding the jury from assessing his defensive reaction against a hypothetical objective baseline.

(e) Kant's belief was reasonable because the controlling inquiry is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist firing, and Bentham's leveled revolver and explicit threat plainly overcome that threshold. **Answer:** (b)

(a) falsely attributes the "reasonable to him" formulation to *Goetz*; the Court of Appeals expressly rejected that Appellate Division reading and retained an objective element. (c) falsely excludes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker; *Goetz* expressly included them. (d) falsely states that NY follows the Model Penal Code; *Goetz* noted that the legislature "crucially inserted the word 'reasonably' before 'believes,'" rejecting the Model Penal Code's purely subjective approach. (e) imports the Model Penal Code duress standard ("person of reasonable firmness" in § 2.09) into a self-defense question; self-defense asks about the reasonableness of the defensive belief, not about firmness in resisting coercion.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, Goetz], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part II (*People v. Goetz*, 68 N.Y.2d 96 (1986): objective standard incorporates defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and knowledge of attacker; rejection of purely subjective Model Penal Code-style test).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that *Goetz* effectively created a subjective standard by permitting the jury to consider the defendant's unique background, prior experiences, and physical attributes. Since the standard is highly tied to what the defendant knew and experienced, one could claim it operates as the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" test in practice, prohibiting second-guessing by a truly external objective standard.
(b) Argument-for: This tracks the exact holding of *Goetz*. The New York Court of Appeals established a hybrid standard: the factfinder must determine whether a reasonable person *in the defendant's situation* would have believed deadly force was necessary. Incorporating Kant's specific knowledge of Bentham's hospitalization threat and the physical realities of the confrontation perfectly models this contextualized objective inquiry.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that the very definition of an "objective" standard requires stripping away the defendant's personal idiosyncrasies and unique knowledge. To prevent the standard from collapsing into subjectivity, a strict objective test must rely only on external facts—the revolver and the distance—while excluding internal elements like Kant's memory of prior threats.
(d) Argument-for: A student might recall that *Goetz* distinguished New York law from the Model Penal Code and mistakenly conclude that New York *adopted* the MPC's purely subjective approach. If New York followed the MPC, the inquiry would end at Kant's honest belief that deadly force was necessary, making his internal fear sufficient to justify the shooting without an objective baseline.
(e) Argument-for: A student might conflate the justification of self-defense with the excuse of duress. Under the MPC's duress standard, the key metric is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist the pressure. Facing a leveled revolver at close range is an immense situational pressure, which could plausibly trigger this standard if mistakenly applied to self-defense.

Head-to-head: Option (b) correctly states the legal standard from *Goetz*, which requires an objective assessment situated in the defendant's precise circumstances, including prior knowledge. Option (a) makes an explicit, false historical and doctrinal claim by asserting *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's strictly subjective standard, which the Court of Appeals specifically reversed. Option (c) relies on a categorical, falsifiable claim that the *Goetz* standard excludes prior experiences, directly contradicting the case's holding. Option (d) falsely claims New York follows the MPC's purely subjective approach. Option (e) explicitly imports the "person of reasonable firmness" test, which is exclusively an element of duress, not self-defense. All distractors are definitively wrong.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "*Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's 'reasonable to him' formulation" — wrong because the Court of Appeals explicitly reversed the Appellate Division and rejected this purely subjective test.
- (c): "the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences" — wrong because *Goetz* expressly requires the jury to consider the defendant's prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker.
- (d): "New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation" — wrong because New York's legislature purposefully added "reasonably," thereby rejecting the MPC's purely subjective approach.
- (e): "the controlling inquiry is whether a 'person of reasonable firmness' would have been unable to resist firing" — wrong because this is the standard for duress, not the self-defense standard for the reasonableness of deadly force.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at Kant's chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. Prior to the robbery, Bentham had explicitly threatened to have Kant put in the hospital for a week. Assume that, whether or not Kant was the initial aggressor, his killing of Bentham proceeded to a jury on self-defense. Under New York's reasonableness standard as articulated in *People v. (a) Kant's belief was reasonable because *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation, which asks only whether the defendant himself regarded his response as warranted. Under this strictly subjective view, as long as Kant genuinely feared Bentham's leveled revolver and remembered the hospitalization threat, the jury cannot second-guess his defensive action using an external, objective standard.

(b) A reasonable person in Kant's situation — facing Bentham leveling a revolver at his chest from six feet, with the only exit fifteen feet away without cover, and with knowledge of Bentham's explicit threat of hospitalizing violence — could reasonably believe deadly force was immediately necessary; *Goetz* permits the factfinder to incorporate the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker in the objective inquiry.

(c) Kant's belief was unreasonable because the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker or the attacker's reputation for violence; those are subjective elements that would convert the reasonable-person test into a defendant-specific inquiry. Consequently, the jury may consider the distance of six feet and the revolver, but must ignore Bentham's explicit threat.

(d) Kant's belief was reasonable because New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation, which asks only whether the defendant believed deadly force was necessary, without requiring objective reasonableness. Under this specific framework, Kant's honest fear of the leveled revolver and prior threat satisfies the justification, precluding the jury from assessing his defensive reaction against a hypothetical objective baseline.

(e) Kant's belief was reasonable because the controlling inquiry is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist firing, and Bentham's leveled revolver and explicit threat plainly overcome that threshold. This test evaluates whether the severe situational pressure of facing a gun from six feet without cover would compel a person to break the law, thereby excusing Kant's lethal response against the aggressor.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly applies *Goetz*, which held that reasonableness incorporates the defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and specific knowledge of the attacker — not a purely external, context-stripped inquiry. The leveled revolver from six feet, the absence of any safe exit, and Bentham's prior explicit threat of hospitalizing violence all inform what a reasonable person in Kant's situation would have perceived. (a) falsely attributes the "reasonable to him" formulation to *Goetz*; the Court of Appeals expressly rejected that Appellate Division reading and retained an objective element. (c) falsely excludes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker; *Goetz* expressly included them. (d) falsely states that NY follows the Model Penal Code; *Goetz* noted that the legislature "crucially inserted the word 'reasonably' before 'believes,'" rejecting the Model Penal Code's purely subjective approach. (e) imports the Model Penal Code duress standard ("person of reasonable firmness" in § 2.09) into a self-defense question; self-defense asks about the reasonableness of the defensive belief, not about firmness in resisting coercion.

## Q15

### Grounding

**Q15.** Based on the confrontation at the crew's warehouse, assume Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity." Jurisdiction A requires retreat before using deadly force, but only if it can be accomplished "with complete personal safety." Jurisdiction B's Stand Your Ground statute imposes no duty to retreat unless the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity." Jurisdiction C imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. Which of the following statements is the most accurate analysis of Kant's duty to retreat under these specific jurisdictional rules?

(a) In Jurisdiction B, Kant maintains his right to stand his ground because the "criminal activity" limitation applies only to crimes perpetrated against the initial aggressor. Because his receipt of stolen proceeds does not legally disqualify him, the fifteen-foot exit route is irrelevant.
(b) In Jurisdiction A, Kant must retreat because the fifteen-foot distance to the exit establishes a physically available escape route, which automatically satisfies the retreat standard. The fact that Bentham has leveled a revolver from six feet away is considered legally irrelevant here.
(c) In Jurisdiction C, Kant must retreat because the jurisdiction's absolute no-retreat rule does not apply to commercial structures like a warehouse. In such non-residential environments, the duty to retreat is reinstated, meaning Kant must attempt the fifteen-foot dash to the open exit.
(d) In Jurisdiction A, Kant has no duty to retreat because a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver cannot be completed with complete personal safety. The imminent threat of being shot legally excuses Kant from attempting to use the physically available exit route. <!-- correct -->
(e) In Jurisdiction B, Kant maintains his right to stand his ground because the warehouse constitutes a workplace, which universally triggers the castle doctrine exception. The fact that he is engaged in criminal activity does not negate his fundamental right to stand his ground at work.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Option (d) correctly applies Jurisdiction A's provided rule. Jurisdiction A requires retreat only if it is available "with complete personal safety." Running fifteen feet across an open floor without cover while facing a leveled revolver fails that standard, meaning Kant has no duty to retreat. Option (a) incorrectly fabricates a limitation to Jurisdiction B's categorical "criminal activity" bar. Option (b) ignores Jurisdiction A's explicit "complete personal safety" requirement, treating mere physical availability as legally sufficient. Option (c) misapplies Jurisdiction C's rule, which the prompt explicitly states applies "in any setting," not just residential ones. Option (e) relies on a fabricated universal workplace castle-doctrine rule to bypass Jurisdiction B's explicit statutory disqualification for defendants engaged in criminal activity.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (Duty to retreat limitations; "complete personal safety" standard; "engaged in criminal activity" SYG exclusions).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Duty to retreat, Ch 22, Refinement `duty-to-retreat-general`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

Option (e) relies on a fabricated universal workplace castle-doctrine rule to bypass Jurisdiction B's explicit statutory disqualification for defendants engaged in criminal activity.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Option (d) correctly applies Jurisdiction A's provided rule. Jurisdiction A requires retreat only if it is available "with complete personal safety." Running fifteen feet across an open floor without cover while facing a leveled revolver fails that standard, meaning Kant has no duty to retreat. Option (a) incorrectly fabricates a limitation to Jurisdiction B's categorical "criminal activity" bar. Option (b) ignores Jurisdiction A's explicit "complete personal safety" requirement, treating mere physical availability as legally sufficient. Option (c) misapplies Jurisdiction C's rule, which the prompt explicitly states applies "in any setting," not just residential ones. Option (e) relies on a fabricated universal workplace castle-doctrine rule to bypass Jurisdiction B's explicit statutory disqualification for defendants engaged in criminal activity.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (Duty to retreat limitations; "complete personal safety" standard; "engaged in criminal activity" SYG exclusions).

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q15.** Based on the confrontation at the crew's warehouse, assume Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity." Jurisdiction A requires retreat before using deadly force, but only if it can be accomplished "with complete personal safety." Jurisdiction B's Stand Your Ground statute imposes no duty to retreat unless the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity." Jurisdiction C imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. Which of the following statements is the most accurate analysis of Kant's duty to retreat under these specific jurisdictional rules?

(a) In Jurisdiction B, Kant maintains his right to stand his ground because the "criminal activity" limitation applies only to crimes perpetrated against the initial aggressor. Because his receipt of stolen proceeds does not legally disqualify him, the fifteen-foot exit route is irrelevant.
(b) In Jurisdiction A, Kant must retreat because the fifteen-foot distance to the exit establishes a physically available escape route, which automatically satisfies the retreat standard. The fact that Bentham has leveled a revolver from six feet away is considered legally irrelevant here.
(c) In Jurisdiction C, Kant must retreat because the jurisdiction's absolute no-retreat rule does not apply to commercial structures like a warehouse. In such non-residential environments, the duty to retreat is reinstated, meaning Kant must attempt the fifteen-foot dash to the open exit.
(d) In Jurisdiction A, Kant has no duty to retreat because a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver cannot be completed with complete personal safety. The imminent threat of being shot legally excuses Kant from attempting to use the physically available exit route. <!-- correct -->
(e) In Jurisdiction B, Kant maintains his right to stand his ground because the warehouse constitutes a workplace, which universally triggers the castle doctrine exception. The fact that he is engaged in criminal activity does not negate his fundamental right to stand his ground at work.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Option (d) correctly applies Jurisdiction A's provided rule. Jurisdiction A requires retreat only if it is available "with complete personal safety." Running fifteen feet across an open floor without cover while facing a leveled revolver fails that standard, meaning Kant has no duty to retreat. Option (a) incorrectly fabricates a limitation to Jurisdiction B's categorical "criminal activity" bar. Option (b) ignores Jurisdiction A's explicit "complete personal safety" requirement, treating mere physical availability as legally sufficient. Option (c) misapplies Jurisdiction C's rule, which the prompt explicitly states applies "in any setting," not just residential ones. Option (e) relies on a fabricated universal workplace castle-doctrine rule to bypass Jurisdiction B's explicit statutory disqualification for defendants engaged in criminal activity.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (Duty to retreat limitations; "complete personal safety" standard; "engaged in criminal activity" SYG exclusions).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under Stand Your Ground statutes with a "criminal activity" exception, courts sometimes interpret this exception to require a causal nexus between the defendant's crime and the need to use self-defense. If Kant's receipt of stolen property is completely independent of Bentham's aggression, the exception might not apply. Thus, a student could argue Kant maintains his SYG rights because his crime wasn't perpetrated against the aggressor or connected to the conflict, making the 15-foot exit irrelevant under Jurisdiction B.
(b) Argument-for: In rigid duty-to-retreat jurisdictions, the mere physical existence of an unblocked exit can trigger the requirement to attempt retreat before using lethal force. A student could argue that because Kant is only fifteen feet from an exit, it qualifies as an available avenue of escape. If the law demands retreat whenever physically possible, the threat level posed by Bentham might be legally irrelevant until the retreat is actually attempted and fails.
(c) Argument-for: The castle doctrine traditionally eliminates the duty to retreat only in a person's home. A student could argue that Jurisdiction C's law is a general Stand Your Ground statute that, despite the prompt's phrasing, still implicitly excludes commercial settings from the absolute protection afforded to residential dwellings. By this logic, the absolute no-retreat rule wouldn't apply in a warehouse, thereby reinstating Kant's duty to retreat.
(d) Argument-for: Jurisdiction A's duty to retreat is explicitly conditional: it applies only if retreat can be completed "with complete personal safety." Kant is facing a leveled revolver from just six feet away and must cross fifteen feet of open ground without cover to reach the exit. Because turning his back to flee under these conditions clearly cannot guarantee his complete personal safety, the law does not require him to retreat.
(e) Argument-for: The castle doctrine generally extends to a person's workplace, granting them the right to stand their ground even in retreat jurisdictions. A student could argue that this fundamental castle doctrine protection operates independently of general Stand Your Ground statutes. Consequently, Kant's workplace status overrides the "engaged in criminal activity" exception, excusing him from the duty to retreat.

Head-to-head: Option (d) is the clear winner as it perfectly applies the explicit rule provided for Jurisdiction A to the facts. The distractors fail because they introduce explicitly false or directly contradictory rules. Option (a) invents a causal nexus requirement out of whole cloth. Option (b) directly contradicts Jurisdiction A's safety requirement by insisting a physical exit "automatically satisfies" the standard. Option (c) directly contradicts the prompt's premise that Jurisdiction C applies "in any setting." Option (e) uses an absolute ("universally triggers") to invent a castle doctrine override for the statutory criminal exception. All distractors are appropriately falsifiable.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the 'criminal activity' limitation applies only to crimes perpetrated against the initial aggressor" — wrong because it invents a categorical limitation not present in the stated jurisdiction or general common law.
- (b): "automatically satisfies the retreat standard" and "is considered legally irrelevant here" — wrong because Jurisdiction A explicitly evaluates "complete personal safety," making the leveled gun highly relevant.
- (c): "absolute no-retreat rule does not apply to commercial structures" — wrong because it directly contradicts the prompt's factual premise that Jurisdiction C's rule applies "in any setting."
- (e): "which universally triggers the castle doctrine exception" — wrong because workplace exceptions are not universal, and the castle doctrine does not categorically override an explicit statutory SYG criminal activity exclusion.

Recommended fix: none
