### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q15.** Based on the confrontation at the crew's warehouse, assume Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity." Jurisdiction A requires retreat before using deadly force, but only if it can be accomplished "with complete personal safety." Jurisdiction B's Stand Your Ground statute imposes no duty to retreat unless the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity." Jurisdiction C imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. Which of the following statements is the most accurate analysis of Kant's duty to retreat under these specific jurisdictional rules?

(a) In Jurisdiction B, Kant maintains his right to stand his ground because the "criminal activity" limitation applies only to crimes perpetrated against the initial aggressor. Because his receipt of stolen proceeds does not legally disqualify him, the fifteen-foot exit route is irrelevant.
(b) In Jurisdiction A, Kant must retreat because the fifteen-foot distance to the exit establishes a physically available escape route, which automatically satisfies the retreat standard. The fact that Bentham has leveled a revolver from six feet away is considered legally irrelevant here.
(c) In Jurisdiction C, Kant must retreat because the jurisdiction's absolute no-retreat rule does not apply to commercial structures like a warehouse. In such non-residential environments, the duty to retreat is reinstated, meaning Kant must attempt the fifteen-foot dash to the open exit.
(d) In Jurisdiction A, Kant has no duty to retreat because a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver cannot be completed with complete personal safety. The imminent threat of being shot legally excuses Kant from attempting to use the physically available exit route. <!-- correct -->
(e) In Jurisdiction B, Kant maintains his right to stand his ground because the warehouse constitutes a workplace, which universally triggers the castle doctrine exception. The fact that he is engaged in criminal activity does not negate his fundamental right to stand his ground at work.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Option (d) correctly applies Jurisdiction A's provided rule. Jurisdiction A requires retreat only if it is available "with complete personal safety." Running fifteen feet across an open floor without cover while facing a leveled revolver fails that standard, meaning Kant has no duty to retreat. Option (a) incorrectly fabricates a limitation to Jurisdiction B's categorical "criminal activity" bar. Option (b) ignores Jurisdiction A's explicit "complete personal safety" requirement, treating mere physical availability as legally sufficient. Option (c) misapplies Jurisdiction C's rule, which the prompt explicitly states applies "in any setting," not just residential ones. Option (e) relies on a fabricated universal workplace castle-doctrine rule to bypass Jurisdiction B's explicit statutory disqualification for defendants engaged in criminal activity.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (Duty to retreat limitations; "complete personal safety" standard; "engaged in criminal activity" SYG exclusions).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Jurisdictions often interpret "criminal activity" exceptions to Stand Your Ground laws narrowly to avoid stripping self-defense rights from minor offenders (e.g., possessors of stolen goods). A student could argue that Jurisdiction B implicitly limits the "criminal activity" disqualifier to violent felonies or crimes directly precipitating the confrontation (crimes against the initial aggressor). Thus, Kant's mere receipt of stolen property wouldn't trigger the statutory exclusion, rendering the physical exit route legally irrelevant.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that in duty-to-retreat jurisdictions, an unobstructed exit only fifteen feet away objectively establishes an avenue of retreat. Under strict applications of the retreat doctrine, the physical existence of a path places the burden on the defendant to attempt escape before using deadly force. Therefore, the physical availability of the door automatically triggers the duty, rendering the subjective or objective threat level "legally irrelevant" to the route's availability.
(c) Argument-for: A student might argue that even absolute no-retreat rules are historically rooted in the sanctity of the home (Castle Doctrine). In many jurisdictions, broad Stand Your Ground statutes are construed to exclude commercial or public spaces when balancing the sanctity of life against the right to stand one's ground. Therefore, in a non-residential warehouse, traditional duty to retreat principles are reinstated, overriding the general rule.
(d) Argument-for: Jurisdiction A explicitly conditions the duty to retreat on the ability to do so "with complete personal safety." Facing a leveled revolver from six feet away makes a fifteen-foot dash without cover inherently and objectively unsafe. The law does not require a defender to risk taking a bullet to the back while attempting to flee. Therefore, Kant is legally excused from using the physically available exit and has no duty to retreat.
(e) Argument-for: The Castle Doctrine historically extends to a person's workplace, creating a fundamental privilege against retreating from one's own business or office. Because the confrontation occurred at the crew's warehouse (a workplace), Kant's right to stand his ground is preserved despite his criminal conduct.

Options (a), (b), (c), and (e) are all flawed because they directly contradict the prompt's stated jurisdictional rules or invent absolute legal doctrines that do not exist. Option (c) directly contradicts the prompt's stipulation that Jurisdiction C imposes no duty "in any setting." - (c): "does not apply to commercial structures like a warehouse" — wrong because the prompt explicitly stipulates that Jurisdiction C imposes no duty to retreat "in any setting."

---

## Q16

### Grounding

**Q16.** Assume that, whether or not Kant prevails on any other defense, he asserts self-defense to justify stabbing Officer Hand inside his Elm Street apartment during the execution of the valid arrest warrant (Facts 10-14). Under common law principles of self-defense and the castle doctrine, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Kant's claim?

(a) The castle doctrine eliminates the duty to retreat from one's home before using deadly force, but it does not transform otherwise lawful police action into unlawful force. Because Officer Hand was executing a valid arrest warrant after proper loudspeaker identification, his entry was lawful. Without an imminent threat of unlawful force, the threshold predicate for any self-defense claim entirely fails. <!-- correct -->
(b) The castle doctrine affirmatively authorizes defensive force against any person breaching the physical threshold of the home, including law enforcement officers executing warrants. Because the doctrine protects the dwelling as an absolute sanctuary, the legality of the officer's initial entry and the warrant's validity are completely irrelevant to a homeowner's legal privilege to repel the physical breach.
(c) The castle doctrine legally permits defensive force here because the residential setting automatically triggers a conclusive presumption of reasonable fear. This in-home presumption dispenses with the traditional requirement to show the entrant posed an actual threat of unlawful force, meaning Kant is legally presumed to have acted in lawful self-defense regardless of the officer's valid warrant.
(d) The castle doctrine is categorically unavailable to Kant because he was the initial aggressor in the earlier warehouse encounter with Bentham. Under the common law, this initial-aggressor status follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations, legally stripping him of the right to assert self-defense or invoke the protection of his dwelling even against a police officer later that day.
(e) The castle doctrine applies to permit Kant's defensive force because the common law recognizes no "lawful entrant" exception to the sanctuary protection of the home. This strictly territorial doctrine allows residents to use physical force against police officers carrying out court orders so long as the confrontation takes place entirely within the physical boundaries of the resident's dwelling place.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly identifies the structural limit of the castle doctrine. The doctrine eliminates a duty to retreat from one's home, but self-defense still strictly requires an imminent threat of "unlawful" force. Officer Hand's entry under a valid warrant is lawful. Without unlawful force, the self-defense predicate fails entirely. (b) falsely asserts the home is an absolute sanctuary against lawful warrants. (c) falsely claims the home setting dispenses with the unlawful force requirement; even statutory presumptions typically require an unlawful or forcible entry. (d) falsely carries initial-aggressor status across completely unrelated, chronologically separate encounters. (e) falsely claims the common law allows force against lawful police action solely because the confrontation occurs inside the defendant's dwelling.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [castle-doctrine, lawful-entrant, initial-aggressor, unlawful-force-predicate], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I (*People v. Tomlins*, 213 N.Y. 240 (1914): castle doctrine eliminates retreat duty but requires underlying unlawful force).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — castle doctrine / unlawful force predicate, Ch 22, Refinement `castle-doctrine-scope` (and `justification-paradigm`). Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Option (a) correctly applies the foundational principle of self-defense: the force being defended against must be unlawful. The castle doctrine only creates an exception to the duty to retreat; it does not eliminate the basic elements of self-defense. Because the officer was executing a valid warrant, his use of force to enter was lawful. Consequently, Kant cannot establish the threshold requirement of facing an unlawful threat, making self-defense entirely unavailable.
(b) Argument-for: A student might choose (b) by confusing early historical conceptions of the home as an "absolute sanctuary" with actual common law doctrine. Under a layman's reading of "a man's home is his castle," one might assume this confers a blanket property-based right to repel any physical breach of the threshold. Therefore, the legality of the entry could theoretically be deemed irrelevant if one assumes the doctrine is purely about territorial inviolability.
(c) Argument-for: Option (c) is attractive because it mimics modern statutory "Make My Day" laws, which sometimes create presumptions of reasonable fear for residential intrusions. A student might assume that under common law castle doctrine, this presumption completely displaces the need to prove the threat was unlawful. If such a conclusive presumption were applied, Kant would be legally justified simply because the confrontation happened in his home.
(d) Argument-for: Option (d) appeals to the initial aggressor doctrine, which generally strips a defendant of self-defense claims. If Kant's earlier actions against Bentham set the entire chain of police response in motion, a student might reason that he bears ultimate fault. The argument would be that his initial criminal conduct conceptually taints his subsequent interactions, precluding him from using the castle doctrine against the police who are responding to his initial aggression.
(e) Argument-for: Option (e) is plausible if a student believes the castle doctrine serves as a strict jurisdictional boundary against all outside interference. One might argue that the common law prioritized the peace of the dwelling above all else, thereby lacking any explicit "lawful entrant" carve-out for state agents. Under this theory, any person—even an officer carrying out a court order—who enters the physical boundaries of the home assumes the risk of physical resistance.

Head-to-head: Option (a) stands cleanly as the correct application of the castle doctrine, properly treating it as an exception to the retreat rule rather than a license to resist lawful police action. The distractors are effectively sealed by absolutist language that establishes explicit legal falsehoods. Option (b) fails by asserting the warrant's validity is "completely irrelevant," directly contradicting the lawful force doctrine. Option (c) relies on a "conclusive presumption" that explicitly dispenses with the unlawful force requirement, which is false under common law. Option (d) incorrectly extends initial aggressor status across separate temporal encounters. Option (e) falsely claims the common law lacks a lawful entrant exception for police officers. All distractors present clear, objectively false legal propositions.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "the legality of the officer's initial entry and the warrant's validity are completely irrelevant" — wrong because self-defense fundamentally requires the threat of unlawful force, making the legality of the entry highly relevant.
- (c): "dispenses with the traditional requirement to show the entrant posed an actual threat of unlawful force" — wrong because common law self-defense always requires an underlying threat of unlawful force, even inside the home.
- (d): "this initial-aggressor status follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations" — wrong because initial aggressor status is specific to the discrete encounter, not a continuous status that bridges separate chronological events.
- (e): "the common law recognizes no 'lawful entrant' exception" — wrong because common law explicitly distinguishes between unlawful intruders and lawful entrants (like officers executing warrants), prohibiting defensive force against the latter.

Recommended fix: none

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q16.** Assume that, whether or not Kant prevails on any other defense, he asserts self-defense to justify stabbing Officer Hand inside his Elm Street apartment during the execution of the valid arrest warrant (Facts 10-14). Under common law principles of self-defense and the castle doctrine, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Kant's claim?

(a) The castle doctrine eliminates the duty to retreat from one's home before using deadly force, but it does not transform otherwise lawful police action into unlawful force. Because Officer Hand was executing a valid arrest warrant after proper loudspeaker identification, his entry was lawful. Without an imminent threat of unlawful force, the threshold predicate for any self-defense claim entirely fails. <!-- correct -->
(b) The castle doctrine affirmatively authorizes defensive force against any person breaching the physical threshold of the home, including law enforcement officers executing warrants. Because the doctrine protects the dwelling as an absolute sanctuary, the legality of the officer's initial entry and the warrant's validity are completely irrelevant to a homeowner's legal privilege to repel the physical breach.
(c) The castle doctrine legally permits defensive force here because the residential setting automatically triggers a conclusive presumption of reasonable fear. This in-home presumption dispenses with the traditional requirement to show the entrant posed an actual threat of unlawful force, meaning Kant is legally presumed to have acted in lawful self-defense regardless of the officer's valid warrant.
(d) The castle doctrine is categorically unavailable to Kant because he was the initial aggressor in the earlier warehouse encounter with Bentham. Under the common law, this initial-aggressor status follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations, legally stripping him of the right to assert self-defense or invoke the protection of his dwelling even against a police officer later that day.
(e) The castle doctrine applies to permit Kant's defensive force because the common law recognizes no "lawful entrant" exception to the sanctuary protection of the home. This strictly territorial doctrine allows residents to use physical force against police officers carrying out court orders so long as the confrontation takes place entirely within the physical boundaries of the resident's dwelling place.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly identifies the structural limit of the castle doctrine. The doctrine eliminates a duty to retreat from one's home, but self-defense still strictly requires an imminent threat of "unlawful" force. Officer Hand's entry under a valid warrant is lawful. Without unlawful force, the self-defense predicate fails entirely. (b) falsely asserts the home is an absolute sanctuary against lawful warrants. (c) falsely claims the home setting dispenses with the unlawful force requirement; even statutory presumptions typically require an unlawful or forcible entry. (d) falsely carries initial-aggressor status across completely unrelated, chronologically separate encounters. (e) falsely claims the common law allows force against lawful police action solely because the confrontation occurs inside the defendant's dwelling.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [castle-doctrine, lawful-entrant, initial-aggressor, unlawful-force-predicate], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I (*People v. Tomlins*, 213 N.Y. 240 (1914): castle doctrine eliminates retreat duty but requires underlying unlawful force).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Option (a) correctly applies the foundational principle of self-defense: the force being defended against must be unlawful. The castle doctrine only creates an exception to the duty to retreat; it does not eliminate the basic elements of self-defense. Because the officer was executing a valid warrant, his use of force to enter was lawful. Consequently, Kant cannot establish the threshold requirement of facing an unlawful threat, making self-defense entirely unavailable.
(b) Argument-for: A student might choose (b) by confusing early historical conceptions of the home as an "absolute sanctuary" with actual common law doctrine. Under a layman's reading of "a man's home is his castle," one might assume this confers a blanket property-based right to repel any physical breach of the threshold. Therefore, the legality of the entry could theoretically be deemed irrelevant if one assumes the doctrine is purely about territorial inviolability.
(c) Argument-for: Option (c) is attractive because it mimics modern statutory "Make My Day" laws, which sometimes create presumptions of reasonable fear for residential intrusions. A student might assume that under common law castle doctrine, this presumption completely displaces the need to prove the threat was unlawful. If such a conclusive presumption were applied, Kant would be legally justified simply because the confrontation happened in his home.
(d) Argument-for: Option (d) appeals to the initial aggressor doctrine, which generally strips a defendant of self-defense claims. If Kant's earlier actions against Bentham set the entire chain of police response in motion, a student might reason that he bears ultimate fault. The argument would be that his initial criminal conduct conceptually taints his subsequent interactions, precluding him from using the castle doctrine against the police who are responding to his initial aggression.
(e) Argument-for: Option (e) is plausible if a student believes the castle doctrine serves as a strict jurisdictional boundary against all outside interference. One might argue that the common law prioritized the peace of the dwelling above all else, thereby lacking any explicit "lawful entrant" carve-out for state agents. Under this theory, any person—even an officer carrying out a court order—who enters the physical boundaries of the home assumes the risk of physical resistance.

Head-to-head: Option (a) stands cleanly as the correct application of the castle doctrine, properly treating it as an exception to the retreat rule rather than a license to resist lawful police action. The distractors are effectively sealed by absolutist language that establishes explicit legal falsehoods. Option (b) fails by asserting the warrant's validity is "completely irrelevant," directly contradicting the lawful force doctrine. Option (c) relies on a "conclusive presumption" that explicitly dispenses with the unlawful force requirement, which is false under common law. Option (d) incorrectly extends initial aggressor status across separate temporal encounters. Option (e) falsely claims the common law lacks a lawful entrant exception for police officers. All distractors present clear, objectively false legal propositions.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "the legality of the officer's initial entry and the warrant's validity are completely irrelevant" — wrong because self-defense fundamentally requires the threat of unlawful force, making the legality of the entry highly relevant.
- (c): "dispenses with the traditional requirement to show the entrant posed an actual threat of unlawful force" — wrong because common law self-defense always requires an underlying threat of unlawful force, even inside the home.
- (d): "this initial-aggressor status follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations" — wrong because initial aggressor status is specific to the discrete encounter, not a continuous status that bridges separate chronological events.
- (e): "the common law recognizes no 'lawful entrant' exception" — wrong because common law explicitly distinguishes between unlawful intruders and lawful entrants (like officers executing warrants), prohibiting defensive force against the latter.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q17

### Grounding

**Q17.** Assume that, rather than experiencing a psychotic delusion, Kant is charged with attempted murder after stabbing Officer Hand because he genuinely but unreasonably mistook Hand's radio for a drawn firearm and believed Hand was about to unlawfully shoot him. Evaluate Kant's eligibility for imperfect self-defense under the California approach (which recognizes that a genuinely held but unreasonable belief negates malice) versus the traditional New York approach (which treats self-defense as an all-or-nothing justification and rejects imperfect self-defense).

(a) Under the California approach, Kant is eligible for imperfect self-defense because his genuine belief in the need for self-defense negates malice; under the New York approach, his claim fails entirely because his unreasonable mistake of fact defeats the justification.

(b) Under the California approach, Kant is ineligible because a belief based on an objectively unreasonable mistake of fact cannot negate malice; under the New York approach, his claim fails entirely because the state treats self-defense strictly as an all-or-nothing justification.

(c) Under both approaches, Kant is eligible for imperfect self-defense because both jurisdictions recognize that any genuinely held defensive belief, even if based on an unreasonable mistake of fact, negates the specific intent and malice required for an attempted murder conviction.

(d) Under the California approach, imperfect self-defense mitigates the attempted murder charge to attempted criminally negligent homicide; under the New York approach, his genuine but unreasonable belief serves as a complete justification and mandates a full acquittal of the charges.

(e) The Supreme Court's decision in *Kahler v. Kansas* requires every state to recognize imperfect self-defense under the Due Process Clause, meaning both the California and New York approaches are constitutionally required to mitigate Kant's charge down to a lesser-included offense.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) is correct because under California law, a genuinely held but objectively unreasonable belief in the need for self-defense negates malice and mitigates attempted murder to attempted voluntary manslaughter. Under the traditional New York approach, an unreasonable belief defeats self-defense entirely, resulting in no mitigation or justification. (b) misapplies the provided California rule, which explicitly allows an unreasonable but genuine mistake of fact to negate malice. (c) misapplies the provided New York rule, which rejects the doctrine. (d) misidentifies the grading effect in California (it mitigates to attempted voluntary manslaughter, not criminally negligent homicide) and misapplies the New York rule. (e) misreads *Kahler v. Kansas*, which held that due process imposes no specific baseline for mental-state defenses and does not mandate imperfect self-defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [22, 23], topics: [imperfect-self-defense, Schuller-genuineness, jurisdictional-variation, Kahler], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part III (*People v. Schuller*, 15 Cal.5th 237 (2023): genuineness-only rule for imperfect self-defense; Part I table: NY rejects imperfect self-defense); Chapter 23 (*Kahler v. Kansas*, 589 U.S. 271 (2020): due-process deference to state choice).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Imperfect Self-Defense, Ch 22, Refinement `imperfect-self-defense` (and Ch 23 `kahler-constitutional-floor`). Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q17.** Assume that, rather than experiencing a psychotic delusion, Kant is charged with attempted murder after stabbing Officer Hand because he genuinely but unreasonably mistook Hand's radio for a drawn firearm and believed Hand was about to unlawfully shoot him. Evaluate Kant's eligibility for imperfect self-defense under the California approach (which recognizes that a genuinely held but unreasonable belief negates malice) versus the traditional New York approach (which treats self-defense as an all-or-nothing justification and rejects imperfect self-defense).

(a) Under the California approach, Kant is eligible for imperfect self-defense because his genuine belief in the need for self-defense negates malice; under the New York approach, his claim fails entirely because his unreasonable mistake of fact defeats the justification.

(b) Under the California approach, Kant is ineligible because a belief based on an objectively unreasonable mistake of fact cannot negate malice; under the New York approach, his claim fails entirely because the state treats self-defense strictly as an all-or-nothing justification.

(c) Under both approaches, Kant is eligible for imperfect self-defense because both jurisdictions recognize that any genuinely held defensive belief, even if based on an unreasonable mistake of fact, negates the specific intent and malice required for an attempted murder conviction.

(d) Under the California approach, imperfect self-defense mitigates the attempted murder charge to attempted criminally negligent homicide; under the New York approach, his genuine but unreasonable belief serves as a complete justification and mandates a full acquittal of the charges.

(e) The Supreme Court's decision in *Kahler v. Kansas* requires every state to recognize imperfect self-defense under the Due Process Clause, meaning both the California and New York approaches are constitutionally required to mitigate Kant's charge down to a lesser-included offense.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) is correct because under California law, a genuinely held but objectively unreasonable belief in the need for self-defense negates malice and mitigates attempted murder to attempted voluntary manslaughter. Under the traditional New York approach, an unreasonable belief defeats self-defense entirely, resulting in no mitigation or justification. (b) misapplies the provided California rule, which explicitly allows an unreasonable but genuine mistake of fact to negate malice. (c) misapplies the provided New York rule, which rejects the doctrine. (d) misidentifies the grading effect in California (it mitigates to attempted voluntary manslaughter, not criminally negligent homicide) and misapplies the New York rule. (e) misreads *Kahler v. Kansas*, which held that due process imposes no specific baseline for mental-state defenses and does not mandate imperfect self-defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [22, 23], topics: [imperfect-self-defense, Schuller-genuineness, jurisdictional-variation, Kahler], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part III (*People v. Schuller*, 15 Cal.5th 237 (2023): genuineness-only rule for imperfect self-defense; Part I table: NY rejects imperfect self-defense); Chapter 23 (*Kahler v. Kansas*, 589 U.S. 271 (2020): due-process deference to state choice).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option perfectly reflects the legal rules provided in the prompt and black-letter criminal law. Under California's approach, a genuinely held but unreasonable belief in the need for self-defense negates the malice element of murder, making Kant eligible for imperfect self-defense. Meanwhile, New York adheres to the traditional rule where self-defense is all-or-nothing, meaning an unreasonable mistake of fact entirely defeats the justification and provides no mitigation.
(b) Argument-for: A student might choose this option if they conflate the general rules for mistake of fact with the specific doctrine of imperfect self-defense. Because an unreasonable mistake of fact often cannot negate general intent, a student might incorrectly assume that California strictly limits imperfect self-defense to non-mistake-of-fact scenarios, rendering Kant ineligible.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue this option is correct by applying the Model Penal Code's logic broadly to both jurisdictions. Since an unreasonable belief genuinely held still conceptually negates the specific intent to commit an unlawful murder, one could theorize that both California and New York must logically recognize this mens rea negation as a basic failure of proof.
(d) Argument-for: This option appeals to students who recall that unreasonable mistakes of fact often result in negligence liability, making a mitigation to attempted criminally negligent homicide seem theoretically sound for California. For New York, a student might mistakenly recall the subjective prong of the *Goetz* standard and conclude that New York focuses heavily on the defendant's genuine belief, leading to a full justification.
(e) Argument-for: A student might choose this option by interpreting *Kahler v. Kansas* as fundamentally protecting mental-state defenses under the Due Process Clause. If a student believes *Kahler* held that states cannot entirely abolish mens rea negations, they might argue that failing to mitigate for an honest mistake of fact violates this constitutional baseline.

Head-to-head: Option (a) correctly applies the rules spoon-fed in the prompt and foundational criminal law. Options (b), (c), and (d) are immediately falsifiable because they contain explicit legal claims that directly contradict the rules provided in the prompt's parentheses (e.g., claiming California rejects imperfect self-defense, or New York accepts it/acquits). Option (e) relies on an explicit, falsifiable misstatement of constitutional law, as *Kahler* actually allowed states wide latitude to define or restrict mental-state defenses. The distractors are completely legally secure, though the prompt operates heavily as a reading comprehension exercise due to the parenthetical definitions.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "Kant is ineligible because a belief based on an objectively unreasonable mistake of fact cannot negate malice" — wrong because it directly contradicts California law (and the prompt), which uses this exact scenario to negate malice.
- (c): "both jurisdictions recognize that any genuinely held defensive belief... negates the specific intent and malice" — wrong because New York explicitly rejects imperfect self-defense.
- (d): "his genuine but unreasonable belief serves as a complete justification and mandates a full acquittal" — wrong because New York requires an objectively reasonable belief for complete justification.
- (e): "The Supreme Court's decision in Kahler v. Kansas requires every state to recognize imperfect self-defense" — wrong because *Kahler* expressly upheld a state's right to limit mental state defenses and did not constitutionally mandate imperfect self-defense.

Recommended fix: none (the question is technically clean and falsifiable, though removing the parenthetical definitions from the prompt would convert it from a reading comprehension exercise into a true doctrinal recall question).
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q17.** Assume that, rather than experiencing a psychotic delusion, Kant is charged with attempted murder after stabbing Officer Hand because he genuinely but unreasonably mistook Hand's radio for a drawn firearm and believed Hand was about to unlawfully shoot him. Evaluate Kant's eligibility for imperfect self-defense under the California approach (which recognizes that a genuinely held but unreasonable belief negates malice) versus the traditional New York approach (which treats self-defense as an all-or-nothing justification and rejects imperfect self-defense).

(a) Under the California approach, Kant is eligible for imperfect self-defense because his genuine belief in the need for self-defense negates malice; under the New York approach, his claim fails entirely because his unreasonable mistake of fact defeats the justification.

(b) Under the California approach, Kant is ineligible because a belief based on an objectively unreasonable mistake of fact cannot negate malice; under the New York approach, his claim fails entirely because the state treats self-defense strictly as an all-or-nothing justification.

(c) Under both approaches, Kant is eligible for imperfect self-defense because both jurisdictions recognize that any genuinely held defensive belief, even if based on an unreasonable mistake of fact, negates the specific intent and malice required for an attempted murder conviction.

(d) Under the California approach, imperfect self-defense mitigates the attempted murder charge to attempted criminally negligent homicide; under the New York approach, his genuine but unreasonable belief serves as a complete justification and mandates a full acquittal of the charges.

(e) The Supreme Court's decision in *Kahler v. Kansas* requires every state to recognize imperfect self-defense under the Due Process Clause, meaning both the California and New York approaches are constitutionally required to mitigate Kant's charge down to a lesser-included offense.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) is correct because under California law, a genuinely held but objectively unreasonable belief in the need for self-defense negates malice and mitigates attempted murder to attempted voluntary manslaughter. Under the traditional New York approach, an unreasonable belief defeats self-defense entirely, resulting in no mitigation or justification. (b) misapplies the provided California rule, which explicitly allows an unreasonable but genuine mistake of fact to negate malice. (c) misapplies the provided New York rule, which rejects the doctrine. (d) misidentifies the grading effect in California (it mitigates to attempted voluntary manslaughter, not criminally negligent homicide) and misapplies the New York rule. (e) misreads *Kahler v. Kansas*, which held that due process imposes no specific baseline for mental-state defenses and does not mandate imperfect self-defense.

## Q18

### Grounding

**Q18.** Assume that at the time of the stabbing, Kant was suffering from a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia) in an active psychotic episode. As established by Dr. Singer's testimony, Kant specifically knew that stabbing Officer Hand was a crime under the penal code, but his delusion convinced him he was morally required to sacrifice the human host to destroy a demon possessing Officer Hand's body. Apply the M'Naghten wrongness prong to Kant's conduct, distinguishing jurisdictions that define wrongness morally from those that define it legally.

(a) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited across all jurisdictions; Kant's violent resistance to a uniformed officer, coupled with Dr. Singer's testimony about his awareness of the penal code, conclusively demonstrates his knowledge that the physical stabbing was an illegal act under the law.
(b) Kant satisfies M'Naghten's "nature and quality" prong because his psychotic delusion fundamentally distorted his perception of Officer Hand as a demon rather than a human; under the M'Naghten standard, the nature-and-quality prong categorically evaluates the defendant's moral understanding of the victim's true identity rather than their baseline awareness of the physical mechanics of the act.
(c) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17) requires a "severe" mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public; a diagnosis of paranoid schizophrenia alone, without that requisite showing of future dangerousness to society, is structurally insufficient to meet the strict federal standard.
(d) Kant is constitutionally entitled to a directed verdict of acquittal because the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Fourteenth Amendment's Due Process Clause strictly requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong as a baseline floor for insanity defenses; states are not permitted to abolish the moral capacity inquiry when evaluating severe psychotic episodes.
(e) Kant satisfies the wrongness prong under M'Naghten's moral-wrongfulness formulation because his delusion rendered him unable to recognize the stabbing as morally wrong, believing it was morally required to destroy the demon; in jurisdictions that limit wrongness to legal wrongfulness, Kant fails the prong because he understood that stabbing Officer Hand was legally prohibited.

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) correctly applies M'Naghten's wrongness prong based on the recognized jurisdictional split. Under a moral formulation, Kant's delusion provided a subjective moral justification, satisfying the prong. Under a legal formulation, he fails the prong because he specifically knew that stabbing Officer Hand was legally prohibited. (a) falsely claims the wrongness prong uniformly demands legal wrongfulness, ignoring the split. (b) states a categorically false legal rule; the "nature and quality" prong evaluates the physical nature of the act (e.g., physically stabbing a living human versus squeezing a grapefruit), not the moral understanding of the victim's identity. (c) fabricates a "specific danger to the public" element for the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act. (d) inverts *Kahler v. Kansas*, which explicitly held that due process does not require states to adopt the M'Naghten wrongness test.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [M'Naghten, wrongness-prong, moral-vs-legal-wrongfulness, nature-and-quality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Part A (M'Naghten's two prongs); Part D (post-Hinckley reforms); Kahler v. Kansas, 589 U.S. 271 (2020) (cataloguing cognitive/moral strains).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — M'Naghten wrongness, Ch 23, Refinement `mnaghten-wrongness`. Rule paraphrased accurately. Distractors are specifically supported by `mnaghten-nature-quality`, `federal-idra-requirements`, and `kahler-constitutional-floor`. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q18.** Assume that at the time of the stabbing, Kant was suffering from a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia) in an active psychotic episode. As established by Dr. Singer's testimony, Kant specifically knew that stabbing Officer Hand was a crime under the penal code, but his delusion convinced him he was morally required to sacrifice the human host to destroy a demon possessing Officer Hand's body. Apply the M'Naghten wrongness prong to Kant's conduct, distinguishing jurisdictions that define wrongness morally from those that define it legally.

(a) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited across all jurisdictions; Kant's violent resistance to a uniformed officer, coupled with Dr. Singer's testimony about his awareness of the penal code, conclusively demonstrates his knowledge that the physical stabbing was an illegal act under the law.
(b) Kant satisfies M'Naghten's "nature and quality" prong because his psychotic delusion fundamentally distorted his perception of Officer Hand as a demon rather than a human; under the M'Naghten standard, the nature-and-quality prong categorically evaluates the defendant's moral understanding of the victim's true identity rather than their baseline awareness of the physical mechanics of the act.
(c) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17) requires a "severe" mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public; a diagnosis of paranoid schizophrenia alone, without that requisite showing of future dangerousness to society, is structurally insufficient to meet the strict federal standard.
(d) Kant is constitutionally entitled to a directed verdict of acquittal because the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Fourteenth Amendment's Due Process Clause strictly requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong as a baseline floor for insanity defenses; states are not permitted to abolish the moral capacity inquiry when evaluating severe psychotic episodes.
(e) Kant satisfies the wrongness prong under M'Naghten's moral-wrongfulness formulation because his delusion rendered him unable to recognize the stabbing as morally wrong, believing it was morally required to destroy the demon; in jurisdictions that limit wrongness to legal wrongfulness, Kant fails the prong because he understood that stabbing Officer Hand was legally prohibited.

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) correctly applies M'Naghten's wrongness prong based on the recognized jurisdictional split. Under a moral formulation, Kant's delusion provided a subjective moral justification, satisfying the prong. Under a legal formulation, he fails the prong because he specifically knew that stabbing Officer Hand was legally prohibited. (a) falsely claims the wrongness prong uniformly demands legal wrongfulness, ignoring the split. (b) states a categorically false legal rule; the "nature and quality" prong evaluates the physical nature of the act (e.g., physically stabbing a living human versus squeezing a grapefruit), not the moral understanding of the victim's identity. (c) fabricates a "specific danger to the public" element for the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act. (d) inverts *Kahler v. Kansas*, which explicitly held that due process does not require states to adopt the M'Naghten wrongness test.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [M'Naghten, wrongness-prong, moral-vs-legal-wrongfulness, nature-and-quality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Part A (M'Naghten's two prongs); Part D (post-Hinckley reforms); Kahler v. Kansas, 589 U.S. 271 (2020) (cataloguing cognitive/moral strains).

### Edge Case Audit

**Q18.** Assume that at the time of the stabbing, Kant was suffering from a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia) in an active psychotic episode. As established by Dr. Singer's testimony, Kant specifically knew that stabbing Officer Hand was a crime under the penal code, but his delusion convinced him he was morally required to sacrifice the human host to destroy a demon possessing Officer Hand's body. Apply the M'Naghten wrongness prong to Kant's conduct, distinguishing jurisdictions that define wrongness morally from those that define it legally.

(a) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited across all jurisdictions; Kant's violent resistance to a uniformed officer, coupled with Dr. Singer's testimony about his awareness of the penal code, conclusively demonstrates his knowledge that the physical stabbing was an illegal act under the law.
(b) Kant satisfies M'Naghten's "nature and quality" prong because his psychotic delusion fundamentally distorted his perception of Officer Hand as a demon rather than a human; under the M'Naghten standard, the nature-and-quality prong categorically evaluates the defendant's moral understanding of the victim's true identity rather than their baseline awareness of the physical mechanics of the act.
(c) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17) requires a "severe" mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public; a diagnosis of paranoid schizophrenia alone, without that requisite showing of future dangerousness to society, is structurally insufficient to meet the strict federal standard.
(d) Kant is constitutionally entitled to a directed verdict of acquittal because the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Fourteenth Amendment's Due Process Clause strictly requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong as a baseline floor for insanity defenses; states are not permitted to abolish the moral capacity inquiry when evaluating severe psychotic episodes.
(e) Kant satisfies the wrongness prong under M'Naghten's moral-wrongfulness formulation because his delusion rendered him unable to recognize the stabbing as morally wrong, believing it was morally required to destroy the demon; in jurisdictions that limit wrongness to legal wrongfulness, Kant fails the prong because he understood that stabbing Officer Hand was legally prohibited.

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) correctly applies M'Naghten's wrongness prong based on the recognized jurisdictional split. Under a moral formulation, Kant's delusion provided a subjective moral justification, satisfying the prong. Under a legal formulation, he fails the prong because he specifically knew that stabbing Officer Hand was legally prohibited. (a) falsely claims the wrongness prong uniformly demands legal wrongfulness, ignoring the split. (b) states a categorically false legal rule; the "nature and quality" prong evaluates the physical nature of the act (e.g., physically stabbing a living human versus squeezing a grapefruit), not the moral understanding of the victim's identity. (c) fabricates a "specific danger to the public" element for the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act. (d) inverts *Kahler v. Kansas*, which explicitly held that due process does not require states to adopt the M'Naghten wrongness test.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [M'Naghten, wrongness-prong, moral-vs-legal-wrongfulness, nature-and-quality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Part A (M'Naghten's two prongs); Part D (post-Hinckley reforms); Kahler v. Kansas, 589 U.S. 271 (2020) (cataloguing cognitive/moral strains).

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q18.** Assume that at the time of the stabbing, Kant was suffering from a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia) in an active psychotic episode. As established by Dr. Singer's testimony, Kant specifically knew that stabbing Officer Hand was a crime under the penal code, but his delusion convinced him he was morally required to sacrifice the human host to destroy a demon possessing Officer Hand's body. (a) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited across all jurisdictions; Kant's violent resistance to a uniformed officer, coupled with Dr. Singer's testimony about his awareness of the penal code, conclusively demonstrates his knowledge that the physical stabbing was an illegal act under the law.
(b) Kant satisfies M'Naghten's "nature and quality" prong because his psychotic delusion fundamentally distorted his perception of Officer Hand as a demon rather than a human; under the M'Naghten standard, the nature-and-quality prong categorically evaluates the defendant's moral understanding of the victim's true identity rather than their baseline awareness of the physical mechanics of the act.
(c) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17) requires a "severe" mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public; a diagnosis of paranoid schizophrenia alone, without that requisite showing of future dangerousness to society, is structurally insufficient to meet the strict federal standard.
(d) Kant is constitutionally entitled to a directed verdict of acquittal because the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Fourteenth Amendment's Due Process Clause strictly requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong as a baseline floor for insanity defenses; states are not permitted to abolish the moral capacity inquiry when evaluating severe psychotic episodes.
(e) Kant satisfies the wrongness prong under M'Naghten's moral-wrongfulness formulation because his delusion rendered him unable to recognize the stabbing as morally wrong, believing it was morally required to destroy the demon; in jurisdictions that limit wrongness to legal wrongfulness, Kant fails the prong because he understood that stabbing Officer Hand was legally prohibited.

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) correctly applies M'Naghten's wrongness prong based on the recognized jurisdictional split. Under a moral formulation, Kant's delusion provided a subjective moral justification, satisfying the prong. Under a legal formulation, he fails the prong because he specifically knew that stabbing Officer Hand was legally prohibited. (a) falsely claims the wrongness prong uniformly demands legal wrongfulness, ignoring the split. (b) states a categorically false legal rule; the "nature and quality" prong evaluates the physical nature of the act (e.g., physically stabbing a living human versus squeezing a grapefruit), not the moral understanding of the victim's identity. (c) fabricates a "specific danger to the public" element for the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act. (d) inverts *Kahler v. Kansas*, which explicitly held that due process does not require states to adopt the M'Naghten wrongness test.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [M'Naghten, wrongness-prong, moral-vs-legal-wrongfulness, nature-and-quality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Part A (M'Naghten's two prongs); Part D (post-Hinckley reforms); Kahler v. Kansas, 589 U.S. 271 (2020) (cataloguing cognitive/moral strains).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that if an individual knows an act is illegal, they possess the minimum threshold of cognitive awareness to be held culpable in most modern legal contexts. Drawing on rigid interpretations of the M'Naghten rule in jurisdictions that explicitly reject moral delusions, the student might conclude that a known violation of the penal code is the uniform baseline for failing the insanity defense's cognitive tests.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that perceiving a person as a demon inherently alters the "nature" of the act, as destroying a supernatural entity is fundamentally different from killing a human being. The argument would assert that "nature and quality" extends beyond mere physics (the physical mechanics of stabbing) to the cognitive categorization of the object being acted upon, meaning a failure to recognize the victim as a human satisfies this prong.
(c) Argument-for: The federal Insanity Defense Reform Act severely narrowed the insanity defense, shifting the burden to the defendant and requiring a "severe" mental disease. A student could argue that because successful federal insanity acquittees face commitment hearings where dangerousness is assessed (18 U.S.C. § 4243), the element of public protection and future dangerousness is structurally intertwined with the strict federal insanity framework.
(d) Argument-for: A student might mistakenly recall *Kahler v. Kansas* as a case that established constitutional baselines for fundamental fairness in criminal law. The argument would assert that the principle of moral culpability is so deeply rooted in Anglo-American history that abolishing the wrongness prong offends substantive due process, making an insanity defense based on moral capacity a constitutional requirement.
(e) Argument-for: This applies the established jurisdictional split regarding M'Naghten's wrongness prong. In jurisdictions defining "wrong" morally, Kant's delusion that the stabbing was morally necessary to save the host from a demon negates his appreciation of wrongfulness. In states strictly defining "wrong" as legally wrong, Kant's knowledge that his conduct violated the penal code prevents him from meeting the standard.

Head-to-head:
Option (e) accurately captures the doctrine and the specific jurisdictional split requested in the prompt. Distractor (a) contains the absolute claim that wrongness uniformly requires legal wrongness across all jurisdictions, which is directly falsified by the existence of the moral-wrongness standard. Distractor (b) explicitly excludes "baseline awareness of the physical mechanics of the act" from the nature-and-quality prong, which contradicts the classic definition of that prong (e.g., strangling a person vs. squeezing a lemon). Distractor (c) incorporates future dangerousness as an element of the insanity defense under IDRA, which is statutorily false. Distractor (d) makes an explicitly false claim about the holding of *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled the exact opposite. Because every distractor contains an explicit, objectively false legal proposition, the question is highly rigorous and requires no edits.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited across all jurisdictions" — wrong because there is a well-known split where many jurisdictions define wrongness morally.
- (b): "categorically evaluates the defendant's moral understanding of the victim's true identity rather than their baseline awareness of the physical mechanics" — wrong because the nature and quality prong specifically and primarily evaluates the defendant's awareness of the physical mechanics of the act.
- (c): "requires a 'severe' mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public" — wrong because the federal insanity defense under 18 U.S.C. § 17 does not include proof of future danger to the public as an element of the defense.
- (d): "Kahler v. Kansas held that the Fourteenth Amendment's Due Process Clause strictly requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong" — wrong because Kahler held exactly the opposite, explicitly permitting states to abolish the wrongness prong.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q18.** Assume that at the time of the stabbing, Kant was suffering from a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia) in an active psychotic episode. As established by Dr. Singer's testimony, Kant specifically knew that stabbing Officer Hand was a crime under the penal code, but his delusion convinced him he was morally required to sacrifice the human host to destroy a demon possessing Officer Hand's body. ## Q19

### Grounding

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." Assume Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect." If Kant's defense attorney compares how this evidence will fare under the Model Penal Code § 4.01 insanity test versus the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17), which of the following conclusions is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would easily prevail because the federal statute, enacted in the wake of the John Hinckley acquittal, expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984. Therefore, Dr. Laplace's explicit clinical finding that Kant was completely "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" perfectly tracks that federal statutory language, granting him a complete defense regardless of his cognitive appreciation of the wrongfulness of the act.
(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both independent prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the law—and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant faces a higher hurdle: he must establish a "severe" mental disease, total inability (not substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature or wrongfulness of the act by clear and convincing evidence, and the volitional prong is unavailable. <!-- correct -->
(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must definitively show he was totally "unable" to appreciate the wrongfulness of his actions or conform his conduct to the law. Consequently, Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is legally insufficient as a matter of law, because mere substantial impairment of cognitive or volitional functions falls far short of the total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires defendants to demonstrate to establish an affirmative defense.
(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act, the procedural and substantive burdens rest entirely on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence. Dr. Laplace's clear, uncontested psychiatric testimony confirming both cognitive and volitional impairments satisfies that uniform evidentiary standard across jurisdictions, meaning Kant will establish an insanity defense without needing to meet a higher "clear and convincing" threshold in federal court.
(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was recently held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that the Due Process Clause fundamentally precludes any affirmative insanity defense that extends beyond the mere negation of mens rea. As a direct consequence of this binding constitutional precedent, the volitional prong—which focuses on the defendant's ability to control their behavior—is therefore no longer legally available to assert in any state or federal court.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The MPC requires only that the defendant lack substantial capacity to either appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs based on Dr. Laplace's findings. The IDRA tightened the federal standard, requiring "severe" mental disease, total inability to appreciate wrongfulness, dropping the volitional prong entirely, and shifting the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states the IDRA adopted the volitional prong; it explicitly eliminated it. (c) falsely elevates the MPC requirement to total incapacity, whereas the MPC only requires "substantial capacity." (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. Kansas*, which permitted states to abolish the affirmative insanity defense but did not constitutionalize that choice or invalidate the MPC prongs.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [mpc-appreciate-criminality, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, volitional-prong], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section C (Model Penal Code § 4.01 substantial-capacity test); Section D (Insanity Defense Reform Act, 18 U.S.C. § 17: severe disease, cognitive-only, clear and convincing); Section on Kahler v. Kansas.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — MPC vs IDRA Insanity Tests, Ch 23, Refinements `mpc-appreciate-criminality`, `mpc-conform-conduct`, `federal-idra-requirements`, and `burden-allocation-post-hinckley`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." Assume Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect." If Kant's defense attorney compares how this evidence will fare under the Model Penal Code § 4.01 insanity test versus the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17), which of the following conclusions is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would easily prevail because the federal statute, enacted in the wake of the John Hinckley acquittal, expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984. Therefore, Dr. Laplace's explicit clinical finding that Kant was completely "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" perfectly tracks that federal statutory language, granting him a complete defense regardless of his cognitive appreciation of the wrongfulness of the act.
(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both independent prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the law—and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant faces a higher hurdle: he must establish a "severe" mental disease, total inability (not substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature or wrongfulness of the act by clear and convincing evidence, and the volitional prong is unavailable. <!-- correct -->
(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must definitively show he was totally "unable" to appreciate the wrongfulness of his actions or conform his conduct to the law. Consequently, Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is legally insufficient as a matter of law, because mere substantial impairment of cognitive or volitional functions falls far short of the total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires defendants to demonstrate to establish an affirmative defense.
(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act, the procedural and substantive burdens rest entirely on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence. Dr. Laplace's clear, uncontested psychiatric testimony confirming both cognitive and volitional impairments satisfies that uniform evidentiary standard across jurisdictions, meaning Kant will establish an insanity defense without needing to meet a higher "clear and convincing" threshold in federal court.
(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was recently held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that the Due Process Clause fundamentally precludes any affirmative insanity defense that extends beyond the mere negation of mens rea. As a direct consequence of this binding constitutional precedent, the volitional prong—which focuses on the defendant's ability to control their behavior—is therefore no longer legally available to assert in any state or federal court.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The MPC requires only that the defendant lack substantial capacity to either appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs based on Dr. Laplace's findings. The IDRA tightened the federal standard, requiring "severe" mental disease, total inability to appreciate wrongfulness, dropping the volitional prong entirely, and shifting the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states the IDRA adopted the volitional prong; it explicitly eliminated it. (c) falsely elevates the MPC requirement to total incapacity, whereas the MPC only requires "substantial capacity." (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. Kansas*, which permitted states to abolish the affirmative insanity defense but did not constitutionalize that choice or invalidate the MPC prongs.

### Edge Case Audit

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." ### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." Assume Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect." If Kant's defense attorney compares how this evidence will fare under the Model Penal Code § 4.01 insanity test versus the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17), which of the following conclusions is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would easily prevail because the federal statute, enacted in the wake of the John Hinckley acquittal, expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984. Therefore, Dr. Laplace's explicit clinical finding that Kant was completely "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" perfectly tracks that federal statutory language, granting him a complete defense regardless of his cognitive appreciation of the wrongfulness of the act.
(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both independent prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the law—and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant faces a higher hurdle: he must establish a "severe" mental disease, total inability (not substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature or wrongfulness of the act by clear and convincing evidence, and the volitional prong is unavailable. <!-- correct -->
(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must definitively show he was totally "unable" to appreciate the wrongfulness of his actions or conform his conduct to the law. Consequently, Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is legally insufficient as a matter of law, because mere substantial impairment of cognitive or volitional functions falls far short of the total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires defendants to demonstrate to establish an affirmative defense.
(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act, the procedural and substantive burdens rest entirely on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence. Dr. Laplace's clear, uncontested psychiatric testimony confirming both cognitive and volitional impairments satisfies that uniform evidentiary standard across jurisdictions, meaning Kant will establish an insanity defense without needing to meet a higher "clear and convincing" threshold in federal court.
(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was recently held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that the Due Process Clause fundamentally precludes any affirmative insanity defense that extends beyond the mere negation of mens rea. As a direct consequence of this binding constitutional precedent, the volitional prong—which focuses on the defendant's ability to control their behavior—is therefore no longer legally available to assert in any state or federal court.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The MPC requires only that the defendant lack substantial capacity to either appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs based on Dr. Laplace's findings. The IDRA tightened the federal standard, requiring "severe" mental disease, total inability to appreciate wrongfulness, dropping the volitional prong entirely, and shifting the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states the IDRA adopted the volitional prong; it explicitly eliminated it. (c) falsely elevates the MPC requirement to total incapacity, whereas the MPC only requires "substantial capacity." (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. Kansas*, which permitted states to abolish the affirmative insanity defense but did not constitutionalize that choice or invalidate the MPC prongs.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [mpc-appreciate-criminality, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, volitional-prong], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section C (Model Penal Code § 4.01 substantial-capacity test); Section D (Insanity Defense Reform Act, 18 U.S.C. § 17: severe disease, cognitive-only, clear and convincing); Section on Kahler v. Kansas.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could defend this by pointing out that the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act was indeed enacted in the wake of the John Hinckley acquittal in 1984. They might erroneously conclude that to "reform" the defense, Congress codified the modern Model Penal Code standard, which includes the volitional prong. Therefore, Dr. Laplace's finding that Kant was "unable to conform his conduct" would perfectly satisfy this assumed federal standard. Under this misapprehension, the student would logically deduce that Kant has a complete defense under the IDRA regardless of his cognitive state.
(b) Argument-for: This is the correct option and relies on a precise understanding of the differences between the MPC and the IDRA. Under the MPC (§ 4.01), a defendant only needs to lack "substantial capacity" in either the cognitive or volitional prong, which Kant meets based on the psychiatrist's findings. Conversely, the IDRA requires a "severe" mental disease, restricts the defense entirely to the cognitive prong (requiring the defendant to be "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness, rather than lacking substantial capacity), and shifts the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. This answer accurately maps Kant's clinical findings to the distinct hurdles of both standards.
(c) Argument-for: A student might argue for (c) by confusing the Model Penal Code with the strict, traditional M'Naghten rule. Since strict cognitive tests often require a defendant to totally lack knowledge of the nature or wrongfulness of their act, the student could assume the MPC maintains this absolute threshold. They would therefore conclude that Dr. Laplace's finding of mere "substantial impairment" is insufficient as a matter of law. This argument rests on the false premise that the MPC strictly requires "total, absolute incapacity."
(d) Argument-for: A student could justify (d) by generalizing the standard burden of proof for affirmative defenses—often a preponderance of the evidence—to the federal system. They might recognize that the IDRA shifted the burden to the defendant post-Hinckley but mistakenly assume the burden was set at a preponderance rather than the higher "clear and convincing" standard. If both jurisdictions merely required a preponderance of the evidence, Dr. Laplace's uncontested testimony would indeed easily satisfy the burden for Kant uniformly across the board.
(e) Argument-for: A student might select (e) by misinterpreting the Supreme Court's ruling in *Kahler v. Kansas*. Because *Kahler* held that the Due Process Clause does not compel states to retain an affirmative insanity defense (allowing states to adopt a strict *mens rea* approach), a student might conflate this holding with a constitutional prohibition. They could argue that the Court explicitly struck down broader tests like the MPC's volitional prong, rendering them categorically unavailable as a matter of constitutional law in any court.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the clear correct answer because it accurately identifies the substantive and procedural distinctions between the MPC and the IDRA. Distractor (a) fails by explicitly claiming the IDRA adopted the volitional prong, which it actually eliminated. Distractor (c) is falsifiable because it claims the MPC requires total incapacity, directly contradicting the explicit "substantial capacity" text of MPC § 4.01. Distractor (d) fails because it asserts the IDRA requires a preponderance of the evidence, whereas 18 U.S.C. § 17 explicitly commands a clear and convincing evidence standard. Distractor (e) is falsifiable because it claims *Kahler* held the volitional prong unconstitutional, whereas *Kahler* merely held that states are not constitutionally required to adopt it. All distractors contain explicit, identifiable false legal claims properly locked by absolute wording.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984" — wrong because the IDRA explicitly eliminated the volitional prong from federal court, restricting the defense solely to the cognitive prong.
- (c): "the total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires" — wrong because the MPC expressly requires only that the defendant lack "substantial capacity," deliberately rejecting a total inability standard.
- (d): "without needing to meet a higher 'clear and convincing' threshold in federal court" — wrong because the IDRA (18 U.S.C. § 17) explicitly imposes a "clear and convincing evidence" standard for the insanity defense.
- (e): "The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was recently held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in Kahler v. Kansas" — wrong because *Kahler* held only that the Constitution does not mandate an affirmative insanity defense, not that existing affirmative defenses (like the MPC test) are unconstitutional.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." Assume Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect." If Kant's defense attorney compares how this evidence will fare under the Model Penal Code § 4.01 insanity test versus the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17), which of the following conclusions is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would easily prevail because the federal statute, enacted in the wake of the John Hinckley acquittal, expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984. Therefore, Dr. Laplace's explicit clinical finding that Kant was completely "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" perfectly tracks that federal statutory language, granting him a complete defense regardless of his cognitive appreciation of the wrongfulness of the act.
(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both independent prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the law—and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant faces a higher hurdle: he must establish a "severe" mental disease, total inability (not substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature or wrongfulness of the act by clear and convincing evidence, and the volitional prong is unavailable. <!-- correct -->
(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must definitively show he was totally "unable" to appreciate the wrongfulness of his actions or conform his conduct to the law. Consequently, Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is legally insufficient as a matter of law, because mere substantial impairment of cognitive or volitional functions falls far short of the total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires defendants to demonstrate to establish an affirmative defense.
(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act, the procedural and substantive burdens rest entirely on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence. Dr. Laplace's clear, uncontested psychiatric testimony confirming both cognitive and volitional impairments satisfies that uniform evidentiary standard across jurisdictions, meaning Kant will establish an insanity defense without needing to meet a higher "clear and convincing" threshold in federal court.
(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was recently held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that the Due Process Clause fundamentally precludes any affirmative insanity defense that extends beyond the mere negation of mens rea. As a direct consequence of this binding constitutional precedent, the volitional prong—which focuses on the defendant's ability to control their behavior—is therefore no longer legally available to assert in any state or federal court.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The MPC requires only that the defendant lack substantial capacity to either appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs based on Dr. Laplace's findings. (a) falsely states the IDRA adopted the volitional prong; it explicitly eliminated it. (c) falsely elevates the MPC requirement to total incapacity, whereas the MPC only requires "substantial capacity." (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. Kansas*, which permitted states to abolish the affirmative insanity defense but did not constitutionalize that choice or invalidate the MPC prongs.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [mpc-appreciate-criminality, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, volitional-prong], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis
